Appearances of Angels and the Reliability of Reports
Razi refutes the doubt that any person or creature could be an angel in disguise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi refutes the doubt that angelic appearances in human form undermine our certain knowledge of everyday objects, and the principle used by verified scholars to accept or interpret reports from truthful informants.

Core Idea
Exceptional reports do not erase our certain knowledge of ordinary realities.

This passage presents a philosophical doubt: if angels can appear as humans, how can we trust that anything is what it seems? Razi responds by articulating a foundational principle: when a truthful informant reports something, we accept it if possible and interpret or suspend judgment if impossible. This principle safeguards certain knowledge from false conjectures.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the doubt
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the argument that if Jibril can appear as a specific person, then any person or even a fly could be an angel.
The doubt is an extreme form of skepticism.
2
Examine Razi's response and principle
Look for the sentence starting with 'أقول' where Razi quotes the verified scholars. Understand the three categories: possible reports are accepted, impossible reports are interpreted or suspended.
This is the core of the lesson.
3
Reflect on certain knowledge
Razi states that 'ilm qat'i is not shaken by corrupt conjectures. Consider how this principle protects the reliability of everyday knowledge and prophecy.
Connect to the broader theme of prophecy and miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أنكم رويتم فى الأخبار أن جبريل عليه السلام كان يظهر فى صورة دحية الكلبي ، وإذا لم يمتنع فى بديهة العقل لم يمتنع أن يظهر في صورة سائر الأشخاص ، فإذا رأيت ولدي فأمله ليس ولدي بل هو جبريل ، بل الذبابة التي طارت في الهواء لعلها ليست ذبابة بل هي ملك من الملائكة ، فثبت أن هذا التجويز ثابت، أن العلم الضروري بعدم ذلك حاصل ؛ فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن البديهة جازمة بهذه الأحكام مع أن جزمها باطل ولما تطرقت التهمة إليها لم يكن حكمها مقبولا إذ لا شهادة منهم (۳)

(3) أقول : المحققون من المسلمين وغيرهم من أهل الملل يقولون كل ما أخبر به مخبر صادق فإن كان ممكن الوقوع حكمنا بصحته وأحلناه إلى القادر المختار ، وإن كان ممتنع الوقوع إما أن ترجع فيه إلى تأويل مطابق لأصول ديننا أو تتوقف فيه ، وإذا تقرر هذا الأصل لم يبق حيرة في موضع بما ذكره أو لم يذكره ، ومن المقرر أن العلم القطعى لا ينقدح بالظنون الفاسدة والأوهام البعيدة الكاذبة .
The fifth: You have narrated in reports that Gabriel, peace be upon him, used to appear in the form of Dihya al-Kalbi. If it is not inconceivable in the primary intellect, it is not inconceivable that he appears in the form of other persons. So if I see my son and consider him [؟], he is not my son but is Gabriel. Rather, the fly that flew in the air perhaps is not a fly but is an angel from the angels. So it is established that this permission is established, that necessary knowledge of its non-existence is obtained. So it is established by these arguments that the primary intuition is certain of these judgments while its certainty is false, and when suspicion has affected it, its judgment is not acceptable since there is no testimony from them. (3) I say: The verified scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) from the Muslims and others from the people of religions say: Everything that a truthful informant reports, if it is possible of occurrence, we judge it to be true and refer it to the powerful free agent (al-qadir al-mukhtar); if it is impossible of occurrence, we either return in it to an interpretation consonant with the principles of our religion, or we suspend judgment in it. When this principle is established, no bewilderment remains in any place regarding what he mentioned or did not mention. And it is established that certain knowledge (al-'ilm al-qat'i) is not dislodged by corrupt conjectures (al-zunun al-fasida) and far, false fancies (al-awham al-ba'ida al-kadhiba).
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage refutes the fifth doubt that exploits reports of angels appearing in human form. The objector argues that if such a transformation is not inconceivable, then any person or even a fly might actually be an angel in disguise, thus undermining our necessary knowledge of everyday identities. Razi responds by stating the principle accepted by verified scholars: when a truthful informant (e.g., a prophet) reports an event, if it is possible, we accept it and attribute it to God's power; if it is impossible, we either interpret it allegorically or suspend judgment. Since this doubt is based on a possible event (an angel appearing in human form) but is then extended to fanciful possibilities that are not reported, it does not affect our certain knowledge. Certain knowledge (al-'ilm al-qat'i) is not disturbed by corrupt conjectures and false fancies.

Key Terms
مخبر صادق
ممكن الوقوع
ممتنع الوقوع
تأويل
علم قطعي
ظنون فاسدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A miraculous event in the past (angelic appearance) licenses universal doubt about everyday reality.
Correction: Such reports are specific and do not justify general skepticism; verified scholars accept possible reports and interpret or suspend impossible ones, thus preserving certain knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the principle stated by the verified scholars (al-muhaqqiqun) regarding reports from a truthful informant?
A) All such reports must be taken literally without exception
B) If the report is possible, we accept it; if impossible, we interpret or suspend judgment
C) Such reports are always false because they contradict reason
D) Only reports from Muslims are accepted

Key Takeaway
Certain knowledge (al-ilm al-qati) is not undermined by far-fetched conjectures based on exceptional reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that certain knowledge is not shaken by corrupt conjectures. Is there a corrupt conjecture—perhaps from a doubtful story or a skeptical thought—that has quietly eroded your certainty about any aspect of your faith? Will you now actively dismiss it as a mere wahm (false fancy) and re-establish your certainty on what is firmly known?
?
Razi says the report of Gabriel's appearance is accepted because it is possible and from a truthful informant. Has there ever been a time when you rejected a true report from Allah or His Messenger because it seemed strange to you, instead of accepting it as possible and trustworthy? How can you train yourself to submit to whatever the truthful Informant says, even when your mind struggles?
?
Imagine you tell yourself: 'This person I love might not be real—maybe it's an angel testing me.' That thought is the very doubt Razi refutes. When you catch such a thought, will you immediately recognize it as a corrupt conjecture and turn back to the necessary knowledge that Allah has placed in your heart? What concrete step will you take the next time such a doubt crosses your mind?

The Necessity of Prophetic Teaching for Salvation
Razi asserts the necessity of prophetic teaching for salvation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that rational proof alone is insufficient for salvation and that prophetic teaching is necessary.

Core Idea
Rational proof leads to God, but only prophetic teaching brings salvation.

Razi has argued that no teacher is needed for rational knowledge of God. But here he clarifies a crucial qualification: while the intellect can prove God's existence, salvation depends on accepting the Prophet's message. In this note, he explains why mere rational belief is not enough.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation to get a feel for Razi's argument.
Notice that Razi begins by acknowledging a point before stating his own position.
2
Identify the key claim
Find where Razi says 'this alone does not suffice' and the condition he attaches to it. What is needed in addition to rational proof?
Look for the phrase 'except if it is accompanied by the teaching of the statement of the Prophet'.
3
Examine the evidence
Razi cites a hadith about fighting until people say 'la ilaha illallah' and two Quranic verses. Why do these support his point?
The hadith shows that verbal confession on prophetic authority is required; the verses command utterance of tawhid.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why many who believed in God were not accepted. What does this tell us about the role of the Prophet in faith?
Razi implies that submission to prophetic authority is integral to salvation, not just intellectual belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وس (1) أقول : هم لا ينكرون استلزام مقدمات إثبات الصانع النتائجها لكن نقول هذا وحده لا يجزى ولا يحصل به النجاة إلا إذا أصل به تعليم لقول النبي لو أمرت أن أقاتل الناس حتى يزولوا لا إله إلا الله ، وكثير من الناس كانوا يقولون بالتوحيد لكنهم لما لم يأخذوا ذلك منه ما كان يقبل قولهم، وأمثال هذا كثيرة مثل و قل هو الله أحد، واعلم أنه لا إله إلا الله ، فأمر بهذا القول وهو العلم وإن لم تقبلوا قوله كفرتم، مع أنهم ←
And (1) I say: They do not deny that the premises of proving the Maker entail their results, but we say this alone does not suffice, and salvation is not attained thereby except if it is accompanied by the teaching of the statement of the Prophet: 'I was commanded to fight people until they say 'there is no god but Allah'. Many people used to say tawhid, but because they did not take it from him, their statement was not accepted. And many examples of this such as: 'Say: He is Allah, One' (Qur'an 112:1) and 'Know that there is no god but Allah' (Qur'an 47:19). So he commanded this statement—and it is knowledge—and if you do not accept his statement, you disbelieve. While they ←
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This is a marginal note or additional comment by Razi. He responds to those who say that rational proofs alone are sufficient for salvation. He agrees that the premises entail the conclusion (God's existence), but argues that mere rational knowledge is not enough; one must also accept the prophetic teaching. He cites the hadith where the Prophet is commanded to fight people until they profess 'la ilaha illallah', implying that verbal confession from the prophet's authority is required. He notes that many polytheists believed in a creator but did not accept the prophet, so their belief was not accepted. He then references verses that command utterance of tawhid. The note ends abruptly, but the point is clear: prophetic teaching is necessary, not just rational inference.

Key Terms
نجاة
تعليم
توحيد
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi contradicts his earlier position that no teacher is needed for rational knowledge of God.
Correction: Razi distinguishes between intellectual proof (which does not require a teacher) and acceptance of prophethood (which involves submission to the Prophet's authority). Both are necessary: reason leads to God, but salvation requires accepting the Prophet's message.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's note, why is rational proof alone insufficient for salvation?
A) Because rational proof is impossible.
B) Because one must accept the statement of the Prophet and adhere to his teaching.
C) Because the atheists deny the premises.
D) Because reason and revelation are contradictory.

Key Takeaway
Salvation requires not only rational knowledge of God but also accepting the Prophet's teaching and uttering the shahada based on his authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life have you satisfied yourself with a merely intellectual belief in God while ignoring specific commands brought by the Prophet? What would it cost you to truly submit your action to his teaching today?
?
If your acceptance of the shahada is genuine, why are there areas where your conduct reflects the belief of 'many people' who said tawhid but did not take it from him? Will you examine one such area this week and align it with prophetic authority?
?
When you say 'La ilaha illallah', do you feel that you are taking it from the Prophet himself, or is it just a phrase repeated by habit? How can you ensure that your confession carries the weight of his teaching in your heart and deeds starting now?

Mutakallimun on Angels: Subtle Bodies Defended
Mutakallimun affirm subtle bodies; objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's view that angels, jinn, and devils are subtle bodies, and how they defend against the objection that such beings cannot act or be unseen.

Core Idea
Angels are real, physical, subtle bodies — not purely spiritual.

The mutakallimun affirm the existence of angels, jinn, and devils as real physical beings, but of a subtle nature. This raises immediate objections: if they are subtle like air, how can they act? If they are dense, why don't we see them? In this passage, we see the mutakallimun's reasoned response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on mutakallimun's claim
Read the Arabic and translation of the first part where the mutakallimun state their position.
2
Understand the objection
The philosophers and early Mu'tazila object based on two possibilities: subtle (like air) or dense. Consider the logic.
3
Follow the mutakallimun's reply
They reinterpret 'subtle' as colorless, not thin, and argue that dense objects need not be seen.
This reply preserves the physicality of angels.
4
Test your understanding
Try to answer the checkpoint question to see if you grasp the rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : في الملائكة والجن والشياطين ) قال المتكلمون : أنها أجسام لطيفة قادرة على التشكل بأشكال مختلفة والفلاسفة وأوائل المعتزلة أنكروها قالوا لأنها إن كانت لطيفة بمنزلة الهواء وجب أن لا يكون لها قوة على شيء من الأفعال وأن يفسد تركيبها بأدنى سبب وإن كانت كثيفة وجب أن نشاهدها وإلا لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال ولا نراها و الجواب لم لا يجوز أن تكون لطيفة بمعنى عدم اللون لا بمعنى رقة القوام سلمنا أنها كثيفة لكن بينا أن إبصار الكثيف عند الحضور غير واجب وأما الفلاسفة فقد زعموا أنها لا متحيزة ولا قائمة بالمتحيز ثم اختلفوا فالأكثرون قالوا إنها ماهيات مخالفة بالنوع للأرواح البشرية ومنهم من يقول الأرواح البشرية التي كانت أبدانها إن كانت شريرة كانت شديدة الإنجذاب إلى ما يشاكلها من النفوس البشرية فتتعلق ضربا من التعلق بأبدانها وتعاونها على أفعال الشر فهي الشيطان وإن كانت خيرة كان الأمر بالعكس . أقول: نقل عن المعتزلة أنهم قالوا الملائكة والجن والشياطين متحدون في النوع ومختلفون باختلاف أفعالهم أما الذين لا يفعلون إلا الخير فهم الملائكة وأما الذين لا يفعلون إلا الشر فهم الشياطين وأما الذين يفعلون تارة هذا وتارة ذلك فهم الجن ولذلك عد إبليس تارة من الملائكة وتارة من الجن وما نقله المصنف ظاهر .
The statement on angels, jinn, and devils: The mutakallimun said: They are subtle bodies capable of taking various forms. The philosophers and the early Mu'tazila denied them. They said: If they are subtle like air, then they cannot have power over any actions and their composition would be corrupted by the slightest cause; if they are dense, we must perceive them, otherwise it would be possible for mountains to be in our presence without us seeing them. The answer: Why is it not permissible that they be subtle in the sense of having no color, not in the sense of thinness of consistency? Even if we grant they are dense, we have shown that sight of a dense thing when present is not necessary. As for the philosophers, they claimed that they are neither extended nor subsistent in an extended thing. Then they differed: the majority said they are essences different in species from human souls. Some say that human souls whose bodies were evil become strongly attracted to similar human souls, attaching to their bodies and helping them with evil deeds—that is the devil; if they were good, the opposite. I say: It is transmitted from the Mu'tazila that they said angels, jinn, and devils are united in species and differ by their actions. Those who do only good are angels; those who do only evil are devils; those who do sometimes this and sometimes that are jinn. Therefore Iblis is sometimes counted among the angels and sometimes among the jinn. What the author transmitted is evident.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's view that angels, jinn, and devils are subtle bodies capable of changing forms, followed by objections from philosophers and early Mu'tazila. The objectors argue: if such beings are subtle like air, they cannot act and are easily disrupted; if dense, we must see them. The mutakallimun reply: subtlety could mean lack of color, not thinness; even if dense, sight of present dense objects is not necessary. The philosophers then claim these beings are non-extended (like spiritual substances) and disagree on their nature: most say they are a different species from human souls; some say they are human souls that become attached to others. The author then adds a marginal note (أقول) reporting that the Mu'tazila considered angels, jinn, and devils to be of the same species but distinguished by their actions. This explains why Iblis is sometimes called an angel and sometimes a jinn.

Key Terms
أجسام لطيفة
التشكل
المتكلمون
الفلاسفة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels are immaterial spirits with no physical form.
Correction: The mutakallimun affirm they are physical but subtle bodies, not purely spiritual.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun's response, what does 'subtle' (لطيفة) mean?
A) Thin like air
B) Having no color
C) Invisible
D) Light in weight

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun defend the physical reality of angels by redefining subtlety and denying the necessity of sight for dense objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that angels are present around you right now as real, physical beings—or do you treat them as abstract concepts? What would change in your behaviour if you were fully convinced of their physical presence?
?
The mutakallimun defended the physicality of angels using reason. In what area of your faith do you feel intellectually challenged? Are you willing to dig deeper to find answers, or do you rely on blind acceptance?
?
Imagine someone tells you that believing in unseen beings is irrational. After studying this passage, can you articulate a rational defence? If you cannot yet, what is stopping you from learning to do so?

Philosophers and Mu'tazila on Angels: Alternative Views
Philosophers deny physicality; Mu'tazila unify species by action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' view that angels are non-physical essences and the Mu'tazila's view that they are humans of the same species differing in actions.

Core Idea
Philosophers strip angels of physicality; Mu'tazila reduce them to moral classifications.

While the mutakallimun affirmed angels as physical subtle bodies, the philosophers denied their physicality entirely and debated their nature. The Mu'tazila took a different approach, seeing angels, jinn, and devils as one species distinguished only by their actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' position
Read the passage from 'وأما الفلاسفة' to the end. Note the two opinions within the philosophers.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila's view
The author reports a Mu'tazilī view where angels, jinn, and devils are one species differentiated by deeds.
This explains Iblis's dual attribution.
3
Reflect on the differences
Compare these views with the mutakallimun's from the first lesson. Notice the varying ontological commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : في الملائكة والجن والشياطين ) قال المتكلمون : أنها أجسام لطيفة قادرة على التشكل بأشكال مختلفة والفلاسفة وأوائل المعتزلة أنكروها قالوا لأنها إن كانت لطيفة بمنزلة الهواء وجب أن لا يكون لها قوة على شيء من الأفعال وأن يفسد تركيبها بأدنى سبب وإن كانت كثيفة وجب أن نشاهدها وإلا لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال ولا نراها و الجواب لم لا يجوز أن تكون لطيفة بمعنى عدم اللون لا بمعنى رقة القوام سلمنا أنها كثيفة لكن بينا أن إبصار الكثيف عند الحضور غير واجب وأما الفلاسفة فقد زعموا أنها لا متحيزة ولا قائمة بالمتحيز ثم اختلفوا فالأكثرون قالوا إنها ماهيات مخالفة بالنوع للأرواح البشرية ومنهم من يقول الأرواح البشرية التي كانت أبدانها إن كانت شريرة كانت شديدة الإنجذاب إلى ما يشاكلها من النفوس البشرية فتتعلق ضربا من التعلق بأبدانها وتعاونها على أفعال الشر فهي الشيطان وإن كانت خيرة كان الأمر بالعكس . أقول: نقل عن المعتزلة أنهم قالوا الملائكة والجن والشياطين متحدون في النوع ومختلفون باختلاف أفعالهم أما الذين لا يفعلون إلا الخير فهم الملائكة وأما الذين لا يفعلون إلا الشر فهم الشياطين وأما الذين يفعلون تارة هذا وتارة ذلك فهم الجن ولذلك عد إبليس تارة من الملائكة وتارة من الجن وما نقله المصنف ظاهر .
The statement on angels, jinn, and devils: The mutakallimun said: They are subtle bodies capable of taking various forms. The philosophers and the early Mu'tazila denied them. They said: If they are subtle like air, then they cannot have power over any actions and their composition would be corrupted by the slightest cause; if they are dense, we must perceive them, otherwise it would be possible for mountains to be in our presence without us seeing them. The answer: Why is it not permissible that they be subtle in the sense of having no color, not in the sense of thinness of consistency? Even if we grant they are dense, we have shown that sight of a dense thing when present is not necessary. As for the philosophers, they claimed that they are neither extended nor subsistent in an extended thing. Then they differed: the majority said they are essences different in species from human souls. Some say that human souls whose bodies were evil become strongly attracted to similar human souls, attaching to their bodies and helping them with evil deeds—that is the devil; if they were good, the opposite. I say: It is transmitted from the Mu'tazila that they said angels, jinn, and devils are united in species and differ by their actions. Those who do only good are angels; those who do only evil are devils; those who do sometimes this and sometimes that are jinn. Therefore Iblis is sometimes counted among the angels and sometimes among the jinn. What the author transmitted is evident.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's view that angels, jinn, and devils are subtle bodies capable of changing forms, followed by objections from philosophers and early Mu'tazila. The objectors argue: if such beings are subtle like air, they cannot act and are easily disrupted; if dense, we must see them. The mutakallimun reply: subtlety could mean lack of color, not thinness; even if dense, sight of present dense objects is not necessary. The philosophers then claim these beings are non-extended (like spiritual substances) and disagree on their nature: most say they are a different species from human souls; some say they are human souls that become attached to others. The author then adds a marginal note (أقول) reporting that the Mu'tazila considered angels, jinn, and devils to be of the same species but distinguished by their actions. This explains why Iblis is sometimes called an angel and sometimes a jinn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels and devils are entirely different species according to all Muslims.
Correction: The Mu'tazila considered them the same species differing in actions, and some philosophers saw devils as human souls.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila as reported, how are angels and devils distinguished?
A) By species
B) By physical composition
C) By their actions
D) By their location

Key Takeaway
Philosophers see angels as non-physical essences, while Mu'tazila reduce them to a moral typology of a single human species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila reduce angels to a mere moral label based on actions. Are you tempted to define spiritual beings (or even people) only by their outward behavior, forgetting their essence? Commit to asking Allah to help you see beyond actions to the reality He has created.
?
The philosophers suggest that your own soul could become a devil or an angel after death depending on your deeds in this life. Does that possibility frighten you enough to change how you live today? Name one habit you will start or stop this week because of this truth.
?
If angels are truly non-physical essences as the philosophers claim, how does that challenge your daily reliance on the physical world? Will you spend time this week contemplating the unseen, even for five minutes a day?

The Mountain Verse: Proof for the Possibility of Seeing God
Proof from Quranic verse with objection and answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first scriptural proof for the possibility of seeing God, based on the mountain verse, and how the objection and answer refine the argument.

Core Idea
A condition's possibility hinges on the essence, not on accidental impediments.

This passage presents a classic kalam debate over the Quranic verse where God tells Moses: 'You shall not see Me, but look at the mountain; if it settles in its place, then you will see Me.' Ash'ari theologians used this to argue that seeing God is possible in principle. The argument hinges on whether the condition (mountain settling) is possible or impossible. The objection raises a temporal nuance, and the answer distinguishes essence from accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the initial argument from the verse: vision is suspended on a possible condition, so vision is possible.
The proof uses the principle that whatever is suspended on a possible is itself possible.
2
Read the objection
The objection argues that the condition is actually impossible because the mountain was moving and could not settle while moving.
The objection analyzes the Arabic grammar of 'in' (if) to show it refers to future.
3
Read the answer
The answer concedes the mountain was moving but distinguishes the mountain's essence (which admits rest) from the accidental motion, noting the verse only mentions the essence.
This defense allows the condition to be possible in itself, even if not actualized.
4
Reflect on the footnote
The footnote critiques the answer, but it is heavily corrupted; focus on the main argument.
Treat the footnote as an additional commentary, not essential for understanding the core proof.
5
Summarize the logical structure
Write a brief summary of the proof, objection, and answer in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها : أن رؤية الله تعالى معلقة باستقرار الجبل وهو ممكن والمعلق على الممكن ممكن فالرؤية ممكنة . فإن قيل لا نسلم أنه علق الرؤية على شرط ممكن بل على شرط محال ، لأنه علقها على استقرار الجبل حال كونه متحركا وذلك محال ، وإنما قلنا أنه علقها على استقرار الجبل حال كونه متحركا لأن صيغة إن إذا دخلت على الماضى صارت بمعنى المستقبل فقوله إن استقر أى لو صار مستقراً في الزمان المستقبل فسوف ترانى ثم أنه في الزمان المستقبل إما أن يقال أنه صار مستقراً أو ما صار مستقراً، فإن صار مستقراً وجب حصول الرؤية لوجوب حصول المشروط عند حصول الشرط فلما لم تحصل الرؤية بالإجماع علمنا أن الجبل لم يستقر وإذا لم يكن مستقراً كان متحركا ضرورة أنه لا واسطة بين الحركة والسكون فإن الجبل حال ما علق الله الرؤية باستقراره كان متحركا، ومعلوم أن استقرار المتحرك حال كونه متحركا محال ، فثبت أن الشرط ممتنع فلا يلزم القطع لجواز المشروط . والجواب : سلمنا أن الجبل في تلك الحال كان متحركا لكن الجبل بما هو جبل يصح السكون عليه والمذكور في الآية ليس إلا ذات الجبل وأما المقضي لامتناع السكون فهو حصول السكون ، فإذا القدرة المذكورة في الآية منشأ لصحة الاستقرار وما هو المنشأ لامتناع الاستقرار فغير مذكور في الآية فوجب القطع بالصحة (۳). (۳) أقول يمكن أن يقال على قوله المذكور فى الآية منشأ الصحة الاستقرار لا لامتناعه، أن المذكور فى الآية هو وقوع السكون في حال النظر إلى الجبل الذي عبر عنه بقوله عز من قائل فإن استقر مكانه لاصحة السكون التي تلزم ماهية الجبل عند عدم الاشتراط بالحركة وتلك الحال تلزم الحركة فلا يمكن معها صحة السكون. و على قوله : وجوب حصول المشروط عند حصول الشرط . واخذة لفظية ، فإن من الواجب أن وجوب علميه المله ، فإن حصول الشرط مطلقا لا يوجب حصول المشروط ، إذا لم المشروط عند حصول شرط به اتم تكن العملة حاصلة أو كانت السكها معوزة لشرط آخر .
First: The vision of God is suspended on the settling of the mountain, and it is possible, and what is suspended on a possible is possible, so vision is possible. If it is said: we do not accept that He suspended vision on a possible condition, but on an impossible condition, because He suspended it on the settling of the mountain while it was moving, and that is impossible; and we only said that He suspended it on the settling of the mountain while it was moving because the form 'in' when it enters upon the past tense becomes in the meaning of the future; so His saying 'if it settles' means 'if it becomes settled in the future time, then you will see Me'; then in the future time, either it is said that it became settled or it did not become settled. If it became settled, then the occurrence of vision is necessary because of the necessity of the conditioned when the condition occurs; so when vision did not occur by consensus, we know that the mountain did not settle; and if it did not settle, it was moving necessarily because there is no intermediate between motion and rest; so the mountain at the time God suspended vision on its settling was moving, and it is known that the settling of a moving thing while it is moving is impossible. Thus it is established that the condition is impossible, so certainty of the permissibility of the conditioned does not follow. The answer: We concede that the mountain in that state was moving, but the mountain, insofar as it is a mountain, rest is possible for it; and what is mentioned in the verse is only the essence of the mountain; as for that which necessitates the impossibility of rest, it is the occurrence of rest; so the mentioned power in the verse is the source for the correctness of settling, and what is the source for the impossibility of settling is not mentioned in the verse, so certainty of correctness is necessary. (3) I say: It can be said regarding his statement that what is mentioned in the verse is the source of correctness of settling not its impossibility: that what is mentioned in the verse is the occurrence of rest in the state of looking at the mountain which is expressed by His saying, 'if it settles in its place' for the correctness of rest that is necessary from the essence of the mountain when not conditioned by motion, and that state necessitates motion so correctness of rest cannot be with it. And regarding his statement: the necessity of the occurrence of the conditioned when the condition occurs, it is a verbal [issue], because it is necessary that... the occurrence of the condition absolutely does not necessitate the occurrence of the conditioned, if the conditioned when the condition occurs is not [complete] or the cause is missing another condition.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the first scriptural proof for the possibility of seeing God, based on the Quranic verse about Moses (Q. 7:143). The proof argues that God suspended vision on the settling of the mountain, which is a possible event; what is suspended on a possible is itself possible, so vision is possible. An objection is then raised: the condition is actually impossible because the mountain was moving at the time, and the past tense 'in' indicates future, so the verse means 'if it becomes settled in the future', but the mountain never settled (since it turned to dust), so it must have been moving, and a moving thing cannot settle while moving. Thus the condition is impossible, so no possibility for vision follows. The answer distinguishes between the mountain's essence (which is capable of rest) and the actual occurrence of rest; the verse only mentions the essence, not the impediment to rest (the motion). Therefore, the condition is possible in itself, and the impossibility of its occurrence in that particular case does not affect the general possibility. The footnote further critiques this answer, suggesting that the verse's mention of 'settling' refers to the occurrence of rest in that situation which is inherently linked to motion, and also challenges the principle that condition occurrence necessitates the conditioned, noting it may be incomplete or have other conditions.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الجبل
الاستقرار
المعلق
الشرط
المشروط
السكون
الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a condition is impossible in a particular case, the conditioned is impossible in principle.
Correction: The possibility of the condition is assessed based on the essence, not on accidental circumstances. The essence of the mountain admits rest, so the condition is possible in itself, even if it did not occur due to motion.

Checkpoint
How does the answer to the objection defend the possibility of vision?
A) By claiming the mountain was not actually moving.
B) By distinguishing the mountain's essence (which admits rest) from the accidental motion, noting the verse only mentions the essence.
C) By asserting that God can make the mountain settle despite movement.
D) By reinterpreting the verse to mean a different kind of vision.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing God is upheld by focusing on the inherent capability of the condition (mountain to rest) rather than on accidental circumstances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your desire to see God in the afterlife, do you base your hope on His possibility (that you can see Him) or on your own actions? Which part of the argument—the condition's possibility or its actual occurrence—do you rely on most, and why?
?
The argument distinguishes between a thing's essence and its accidental circumstances. In your own spiritual life, are you focusing on the essence of your faith (what is possible for you) or on temporary obstacles that make you feel distant from God? What would change if you shifted your focus to the essence?
?
If you were to ask God for something as Prophet Musa asked to see Him, would you be willing to accept God's answer even if it comes with a condition that seems impossible? How does this affect your trust in God's wisdom?

The Proof from Moses's Request for Vision
Moses's request as proof for permissibility of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet Moses's request to see Allah serves as a scriptural proof for the permissibility of vision (ru'ya) in the afterlife, based on prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Moses's request proves vision is permissible because prophets never ask for what is impossible or wrong.

This lesson examines a concise scriptural proof used by theologians to argue that seeing Allah is rationally permissible. The proof draws on the story of Prophet Moses asking to see Allah, as mentioned in the Quran. The argument hinges on the principle that prophets are infallible and would never request something that is either a sin or based on ignorance. By studying this passage, you will see how a simple historical event becomes a theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Note the conditional structure: 'if ... then ...'.
The Arabic is concise, so read it slowly.
2
Understand the logical inference
Identify the two possibilities the author eliminates (error or ignorance) and why both are impossible for a prophet.
This step is crucial for grasping the proof.
3
Consider the underlying principle
Reflect on why prophetic infallibility ('isma) is assumed. This is a key doctrine in Sunni theology.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Some think Moses asked for a spiritual insight, not literal vision. Why does the proof assume literal vision?
The argument works only if the request is literal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : أن موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام سأل الرؤية ولو لم تكن الرؤية جائزة لكان سؤال موسى عينا أو جهلا .
Second: That Moses, peace be upon him, asked for vision; and if vision were not permissible, then Moses's asking would be either an error or ignorance.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the second scriptural proof for the possibility of seeing God. It is a brief argument: Moses, a prophet, asked to see God. If seeing God were impossible or impermissible, then Moses's request would be either a sin (error) or a sign of ignorance—both are impossible for a prophet. Therefore, vision must be permissible. The proof relies on the principle that prophets are infallible and do not ask for what is impossible or inappropriate.

Key Terms
موسى
سؤال
الرؤية
جائزة
عينا
جهلا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Moses's request was merely for a miracle or spiritual insight, not literal vision.
Correction: The argument assumes the request was for literal vision because the Arabic term 'ru'ya' and the context indicate a physical seeing. If it were metaphorical, the conditional would not hold.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider Moses's request as proof that vision is permissible?
A) Because God granted Moses's request immediately.
B) Because Moses is a prophet and would not ask for something impermissible or impossible.
C) Because the Quran explicitly says vision is possible.
D) Because all prophets asked for vision.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic infallibility implies that Moses's request for vision could only be for something permissible, thus establishing that seeing Allah is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah for something in your prayers, do you ever fear that your request might be based on ignorance of what is truly good for you? How does Moses's confidence in asking for vision challenge your own level of certainty about what is permissible to ask of Allah?
?
Moses's request was based on a deep longing to see Allah. Do you truly long to see Allah as intensely? If not, what is holding you back from that desire?
?
The proof assumes that prophets never err or act ignorantly. Are there areas in your life where you make excuses for your own mistakes by claiming ignorance? How does the high standard of prophetic infallibility call you to a higher level of accountability?

The Second Proof: Moses’ Request for Vision
Razi argues that Moses' request to see Allah implies the possibility of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash‘ari theologians consider Prophet Moses’ request to see Allah a rational proof for the possibility of vision (ru’ya).

Core Idea
Prophets cannot ask for the impossible – Moses’ request proves vision is possible.

This lesson examines the second proof for the possibility of seeing Allah found in Razi's Muhaṣṣal. The argument draws on the prophetic request of Moses (peace be upon him) recorded in the Quran. How can a request that seems to ask for the impossible actually serve as evidence? Razi shows that the very act of asking, given prophetic infallibility, implies that vision is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument is presented as a conditional statement.
2
Understand the logical structure
The argument is: if vision were not possible, then Moses' request would be either frivolous (an unnecessary request) or ignorant (not knowing it is impossible). Both are impossible for a prophet. Therefore, vision must be possible.
Identify the premise that prophets are infallible (ma'sum).
3
Recognize the key terms
Focus on the Arabic terms: سأل (asked), الرؤية (vision), عبث (frivolous), جهل (ignorant). Each term carries theological weight.
4
Reflect on prophetic infallibility
Why is it impossible for a prophet to ask for something impossible? Consider the doctrine of 'isma (infallibility) and how it underpins this proof.
5
Formulate the proof in your own words
Try to rewrite the argument as a clear syllogism: Major premise, minor premise, conclusion.
This helps internalize the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها: أن موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام سأل الرؤية ولو لم تكن الرؤية جائزة لكان سؤال موسى عبثا أو جهلا.
And the second: that Moses, peace and blessings be upon him, asked for the vision; and if vision were not permissible, then Moses' request would be frivolous or ignorant.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the possibility of seeing Allah: the fact that Prophet Moses (peace be upon him) asked Allah to show Himself. Moses is a prophet and infallible (ma'sum); he would not ask for something impossible or meaningless. His request indicates that vision is permissible in itself. The argument is concise: if vision were impossible, Moses' request would be either frivolous (an unnecessary request) or ignorant (not knowing it is impossible), both of which are impossible for a prophet. Therefore, vision must be possible.

Key Terms
سأل
الرؤية
عبث
جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Moses' request was a test or that he was not aware of the impossibility.
Correction: The Ash'ari argument relies on prophetic infallibility: a prophet cannot be frivolous or ignorant, so his request demonstrates that vision is permissible in itself.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider Moses' request a proof for the possibility of seeing Allah?
A) Because all prophets are infallible and would not ask for the impossible.
B) Because Moses was a great prophet and his request was granted.
C) Because the Quran records the request without criticism.
D) Because vision is necessary for Moses to receive revelation.

Key Takeaway
Moses’ request for vision, given his infallibility, provides a rational proof that seeing Allah is possible in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that prophets are infallible. Does your own du'a reflect the same certainty that what you ask for is possible with Allah, or do you sometimes ask with hidden doubt? How can you cultivate the same boldness as Moses in requesting spiritual closeness?
?
If seeing Allah in the afterlife is truly possible, how does that reality reshape your longing for Paradise? Are you actively preparing your heart to witness His beauty, or is your vision of Jannah still focused on its worldly pleasures?
?
Moses asked for something that many thought impossible. When you face doubts about Allah's ability to answer your prayers or about the truths of the unseen, do you submit to those doubts or trust in the infallible example of the prophets? What will you do this week to strengthen your certainty in what Allah has promised?

The Eighth Argument: Prophets' Confessions of Sins
Mu'tazilites cite prophets' confessions to argue for human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilites use the prophets' own confessions of sin as evidence that humans, not God, are the true agents of their actions.

Core Idea
Prophets confessed their sins to themselves, not to God, proving human agency.

The Mu'tazilites turn to the Quran itself, citing verses where even the most righteous humans—the prophets—confess their sins and attribute their actions to themselves. If God were the sole creator of all actions, why would they say 'we wronged ourselves'? This passage presents the eighth argument for free will, grounding it in the words of Adam, Jonah, Moses, Jacob, and Noah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Note each prophet and the specific wording of their confession.
The verbs indicate self-attribution: ظلمنا, ظلمت, سولت, etc.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite reasoning
The Mu'tazilites argue that if God created all actions, the prophets would not blame themselves; they would attribute the sin to God. Instead, they say 'we wronged ourselves'.
This implies the prophets were the doers (فاعلين) of their actions.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider the opposing view: if God creates sin, does it make sense for a prophet to confess it as his own? The Mu'tazilites say no, thus humans must have free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن: الآيات الدالة على اعتراف الأنبياء بذنوبهم وإضافتهم إلى أنفسهم، كقوله تعالى حكاية عن آدم: {ربنا ظلمنا أنفسنا} [الأعراف:23]، وعن يونس: {سبحانك إني كنت من الظالمين} [الأنبياء:87]، وعن موسى: {رب إني ظلمت نفسي} [القصص:16]، وقال يعقوب لأولاده: {بل سولت لكم أنفسكم} [يوسف:83]، وقال: {من بعد أن نزع الشيطان بيني وبين إخوتي} [يوسف:100]، وقال نوح: {رب إني أعوذ بك أن أسألك ما ليس لي به علم} [هود:47]. قالوا: فهذه الآيات دالة على اعتراف الأنبياء بكونهم فاعلين لأفعالهم.
The eighth: Verses indicating the confession of the prophets of their sins and attributing them to themselves, such as His saying, narrating from Adam: 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves.' (al-A'raf 23), and from Jonah: 'Exalted are You; indeed, I have been of the wrongdoers.' (al-Anbiya 87), and from Moses: 'My Lord, indeed I have wronged myself.' (al-Qasas 16), and Jacob said to his sons: 'Rather, your souls have enticed you to something.' (Yusuf 83), and he said: 'After Satan had cast [animosity] between me and my brothers.' (Yusuf 100), and Noah said: 'My Lord, indeed I seek refuge in You from asking that of which I have no knowledge.' (Hud 47). They said: These verses indicate the prophets' confession that they are doers of their actions.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument, which cites Quranic verses where prophets confess their sins or attribute actions to themselves. The Mu'tazilites argue that if God were the sole creator of actions, the prophets would not attribute sins to themselves; they would attribute them to God. By saying 'we have wronged ourselves', they acknowledge their own agency. This proves that humans are the true agents of their actions, including sinful ones.

Key Terms
اعتراف
الأنبياء
فاعل
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets' confessions are merely verbal expressions and do not imply real agency or free will.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites argue that the prophets would not genuinely confess and attribute sins to themselves if they were not truly responsible; their speech reflects their actual capacity and agency.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazilites cite the prophets' confessions as evidence for free will?
A) Because prophets never sin.
B) Because the prophets attribute their sins to themselves, not to God.
C) Because the prophets deny responsibility.
D) Because the prophets ask for forgiveness.

Key Takeaway
The prophets' direct attribution of sins to themselves serves as evidence that humans are the agents of their actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly own it as your own action, or do you subtly blame circumstances, your upbringing, or even God’s decree? How does the prophets' example of direct self-attribution challenge you to take full responsibility today?
?
The prophets confessed their sins openly. Is there a sin you have been hiding or justifying? What would it cost you to confess it to God as they did, without shifting blame?
?
If you believe that every action is from God (jabr), then why did the prophets blame themselves? How does this lesson force you to reconsider your own view of human responsibility?

The First Proof: Claim and the Quranic Miracle
Razi presents the first proof: claim + Quran miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof of Muhammad's prophethood based on his claim and the miracle of the Quran, including the concept of tahaddi (challenge).

Core Idea
A claim to prophethood backed by a miracle is proof of prophethood.

Imam Razi begins his argument for Muhammad's prophethood by establishing a universal principle: anyone who claims prophethood and performs a miracle is a true prophet. The Quran itself is the greatest evidence of this principle, as it challenges the most eloquent to produce its like, and they failed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first proof
Focus on the first paragraph: from 'وأنما قلنا أنه أتى بالقرآن' to 'فعجزوا عنه، وذلك يدل على كونه معجزاً'. Notice how the principle is stated and then applied to the Quran.
The principle is that a claim plus miracle equals prophethood.
2
Understand the principle
Identify the general rule: 'كل من كان كذلك كان نبيا'. Then see how it applies to Muhammad: he claimed prophethood (proven by tawatur) and performed the miracle of the Quran.
The Quran's miracle is its inimitability (i'jaz).
3
Analyze the Quranic challenge
The text says the Quran is a miracle because he challenged the eloquent (fuhaha) to oppose it and they were unable. Reflect on why this inability is proof.
This is the 'tahaddi' argument.
4
Reflect on the learning objective
Consider how this passage establishes the first proof as a valid argument against those who deny Muhammad's prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : محمد رسول الله خلافا لليهود والنصارى والمجوس وجماعة من الدهرية لنا وجوه ) الأول: أنه ادعى النبوة وعليه التعويل وظهر المعجز عليه وكل من كان كذلك كان نبيا وأنما قلنا أنه ادعى النبوة فالتواتر وإنما قلنا أنه ظهر المعجز على يده فلثلاثة أوجه أحدها أنه أتى بالقرآن والقرآن معجز أما أنه أتى بالقرآن ولم يأت به غيره في التواتر وأما أنه معجز فلأنه تحدى الفصحاء بمعارضته فعجزوا عنه ، وذلك يدل على كونه معجزاً . وثانيها : أنه نقل عنه معجزات كثيرة منها إشباع الخلق الكثير من الطعام القليل ونبوع الماء من بين أصابعه ومكالمة الحيوان العجم ، وكل واحد منها وإن لم يبلغ مبلع التواتر لكن التواتر يدل على صحة واحد منها وأى واحد منها صح حصل الغرض . وثالثها : أنه أخبر عن الغيب والإخبار عن الغيب معجز ، و إنما قلنا إن من ادعى النبوة وظهر المعجز عليه كان نبيا لأن الرجل إذا قام في المحفل العظيم فقال إلى رسول هذا الملك إليكم ثم قال يا أيها الملك إن كنت صادقا فيما قلت فخالف عادتك وقم عن مكانك ، فمتى قام الملك أضطر الحاضرون إلى صدقه فكذا هنا الطريق الثاني : إثبات نبوته عليه السلام بالاستدلال بأخلاقه وأفعاله وأحكامه وسيره فإن كل واحد منها وإن كان لا يدل على النبوة السكن مجموعها مما يعلم قطعاً أنه لا يحصل إلا للانبياء وهذه طريقة أختارها الجاحظ وارتضى بها الغزالي في كتابه المنقذ . الثالث : إخبار الأنبياء المتقدمين في كتبهم السماوية عن نبوته فهذا مجامع أدلة نبوته عليه السلام والاستقصاء فيها مذكور في المطولات
Question: Muhammad is the Messenger of God, contrary to the Jews, Christians, Magians, and a group of materialists. For us there are [several] ways: First: He claimed prophethood—and reliance is upon this—and a miracle appeared upon him, and whoever is like that is a prophet. We said he claimed prophethood—by tawatur; we said a miracle appeared at his hand—by three aspects: One is that he brought the Quran, and the Quran is a miracle. As for he brought the Quran and no one else brought it, it is by tawatur; as for it being a miracle, because he challenged the eloquent to oppose it and they were unable, and that indicates it is a miracle. Second: Many miracles are transmitted from him, such as feeding a large multitude from a small amount of food, water gushing from between his fingers, and speaking to non-human animals. Although each of them does not reach the level of tawatur, tawatur indicates the soundness of one of them, and whichever is sound achieves the purpose. Third: He informed of the unseen, and informing of the unseen is a miracle. We said that whoever claims prophethood and a miracle appears upon him is a prophet because, if a man stands in a great assembly and says, 'I am a messenger of this king to you,' then says, 'O king, if I am truthful in what I say, then break your custom and stand from your place,' then when the king stands, the attendees are compelled to believe him. So is the case here. The second way: Proving his prophethood—peace be upon him—by deducing from his character, actions, judgments, and conduct. For each one of them, although it does not indicate prophethood alone, yet their totality makes it known with certainty that they only occur for prophets. This is a method chosen by al-Jahiz and approved by al-Ghazali in his book al-Munqidh. The third: The information of the previous prophets in their heavenly books about him. So this is the sum of the proofs of his prophethood—peace be upon him—and detailed discussion is in the longer works.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage provides three ways (later called three 'wujuh' or 'turuq') to prove Muhammad's prophethood. The first way is through miracles: (a) the Quran itself is a miracle due to its inimitability; (b) other miracles like feeding many, water from fingers, speaking to animals—though individually not mutawatir, collectively they are; (c) foretelling the unseen. Then he gives an analogy: if a man in a court claims to be a messenger and calls the king to break custom, and the king does so, people must believe. The second way is holistic: looking at Muhammad's character, actions, and law; though each taken alone is not proof, the totality indicates prophethood. This method is attributed to al-Jahiz and al-Ghazali. The third way is prophecy from previous scriptures.

Key Terms
نبوة
معجز
تحدي
إعجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran is a miracle only because of its content or style, not because of the challenge.
Correction: Razi specifically argues that the miracle is proven by the challenge (تحدى الفصحاء) and their inability, not just intrinsic beauty.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why is the Quran considered a miracle?
A) Because it is long and detailed.
B) Because the eloquent were challenged to oppose it and failed.
C) Because it was revealed over 23 years.
D) Because it contains scientific facts.

Key Takeaway
The first proof of Muhammad's prophethood is that he claimed prophethood and performed the miracle of the Quran, which challenged the eloquent and proved its divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof that Muhammad is a prophet depends on a claim and a miracle. Is your conviction based on having personally examined this proof, or do you rely on inherited belief? If you are not sure, what step will you take today to investigate?
?
If you truly believed that the Quran is a miracle from God that challenges you personally to submit to its message, how would your relationship with it change starting today? What specific verse will you read and obey this week?
?
The eloquent Arabs were unable to produce anything like the Quran despite their best efforts. In what area of your life are you still relying on your own eloquence or ability rather than submitting to God's word? What would it look like to surrender that area today?

First Proof: Other Miracles and Foretelling the Unseen
Razi lists other miracles and gives an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third aspects of the first proof: the other miracles transmitted from Muhammad and his foretelling of the unseen, along with the analogy that establishes the connection between miracle and prophethood.

Core Idea
Multiple miracles, though individually not mutawatir, collectively confirm prophethood.

Imam Razi continues the first proof by listing other miracles beyond the Quran, such as feeding many from little food and water flowing from fingers. He also mentions Muhammad's prophecies about the unseen, which themselves are miracles. Then he gives a vivid analogy to explain why a miracle combined with a claim compels belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on other miracles
From 'وثانيها : أنه نقل عنه معجزات كثيرة' to 'حصل الغرض'. Notice the examples and the argument about tawatur.
The miracles are not individually mutawatir, but collectively they are.
2
Understand the foretelling argument
Read 'وثالثها : أنه أخبر عن الغيب والإخبار عن الغيب معجز'. Consider why prophecy is a miracle.
Foretelling the unseen implies divine knowledge.
3
Analyze the analogy
Read the analogy from 'و إنما قلنا إن من ادعى النبوة' to 'فكذا هنا'. Understand how it illustrates the necessity of belief.
The king breaking his custom represents a miracle.
4
Connect to the overall proof
See how these aspects together strengthen the first proof beyond just the Quran.

مسألة : محمد رسول الله خلافا لليهود والنصارى والمجوس وجماعة من الدهرية لنا وجوه ) الأول: أنه ادعى النبوة وعليه التعويل وظهر المعجز عليه وكل من كان كذلك كان نبيا وأنما قلنا أنه ادعى النبوة فالتواتر وإنما قلنا أنه ظهر المعجز على يده فلثلاثة أوجه أحدها أنه أتى بالقرآن والقرآن معجز أما أنه أتى بالقرآن ولم يأت به غيره في التواتر وأما أنه معجز فلأنه تحدى الفصحاء بمعارضته فعجزوا عنه ، وذلك يدل على كونه معجزاً . وثانيها : أنه نقل عنه معجزات كثيرة منها إشباع الخلق الكثير من الطعام القليل ونبوع الماء من بين أصابعه ومكالمة الحيوان العجم ، وكل واحد منها وإن لم يبلغ مبلع التواتر لكن التواتر يدل على صحة واحد منها وأى واحد منها صح حصل الغرض . وثالثها : أنه أخبر عن الغيب والإخبار عن الغيب معجز ، و إنما قلنا إن من ادعى النبوة وظهر المعجز عليه كان نبيا لأن الرجل إذا قام في المحفل العظيم فقال إلى رسول هذا الملك إليكم ثم قال يا أيها الملك إن كنت صادقا فيما قلت فخالف عادتك وقم عن مكانك ، فمتى قام الملك أضطر الحاضرون إلى صدقه فكذا هنا الطريق الثاني : إثبات نبوته عليه السلام بالاستدلال بأخلاقه وأفعاله وأحكامه وسيره فإن كل واحد منها وإن كان لا يدل على النبوة السكن مجموعها مما يعلم قطعاً أنه لا يحصل إلا للانبياء وهذه طريقة أختارها الجاحظ وارتضى بها الغزالي في كتابه المنقذ . الثالث : إخبار الأنبياء المتقدمين في كتبهم السماوية عن نبوته فهذا مجامع أدلة نبوته عليه السلام والاستقصاء فيها مذكور في المطولات
Question: Muhammad is the Messenger of God, contrary to the Jews, Christians, Magians, and a group of materialists. For us there are [several] ways: First: He claimed prophethood—and reliance is upon this—and a miracle appeared upon him, and whoever is like that is a prophet. We said he claimed prophethood—by tawatur; we said a miracle appeared at his hand—by three aspects: One is that he brought the Quran, and the Quran is a miracle. So is the case here. The second way: Proving his prophethood—peace be upon him—by deducing from his character, actions, judgments, and conduct. For each one of them, although it does not indicate prophethood alone, yet their totality makes it known with certainty that they only occur for prophets. This is a method chosen by al-Jahiz and approved by al-Ghazali in his book al-Munqidh. The third: The information of the previous prophets in their heavenly books about him. So this is the sum of the proofs of his prophethood—peace be upon him—and detailed discussion is in the longer works.

The Second and Third Proofs: Holistic Character and Previous Scriptures
Razi presents the second and third proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third proofs of Muhammad's prophethood: the holistic argument from his character and actions, and the argument from previous scriptures prophesying his coming.

Core Idea
The totality of a prophet's character and the testimony of earlier scriptures confirm his prophethood.

After the miracle-based proof, Imam Razi presents two additional arguments. The second proof looks at Muhammad's life, character, and law as a whole, arguing that such a complete package cannot come from a false prophet. The third proof cites the prophecies of earlier prophets in their scriptures, which foretold Muhammad's arrival.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second proof
From 'الطريق الثاني : إثبات نبوته' to 'في كتابه المنقذ'. Notice the emphasis on totality (مجموعها).
Each quality alone is not proof, but together they are compelling.
2
Understand the holistic argument
Consider why the combination of character, actions, judgments, and conduct could only come from a true prophet.
This method was favored by al-Jahiz and al-Ghazali.
3
Read the third proof
From 'الثالث : إخبار الأنبياء المتقدمين' to the end. This is a brief statement, but it points to a longer discussion.
The third proof relies on previous scriptures.
4
Reflect on the overall structure
Understand how these two proofs complement the first one, providing a multi-faceted case for prophethood.

Objection: Are Non-Quranic Miracles Reliably Transmitted?
Objection to miracles lacking mutawatir transmission and the reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that miracles other than the Quran were not transmitted by tawatur and the response that ahad reports do not yield certainty.

Core Idea
Miracles must be mutawatir to prove prophethood; ahad reports are insufficient.

This lesson examines a key objection raised against the miracles of Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). Critics argue that many reported miracles, like feeding a multitude, were only transmitted through isolated reports (ahad) and therefore cannot be accepted as certain. The response concedes this point but redirects attention to the Quran as the primary continuous miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage that presents the objection and the reply. Identify the two distinct complaints and how they are addressed.
The reply references a detailed discussion in another book (al-Nihaya).
2
Understand tawatur vs ahad
Focus on the concept of tawatur (mass transmission that yields certain knowledge) versus ahad (isolated reports that do not). The objection uses this distinction to challenge the reliability of the reported miracles.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If these miracles are not mutawatir, does that undermine the proof of prophethood? The response suggests that the Quran itself remains the central miracle.
This connects to the broader argument for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل لا نسلم أنه ظهر المعجز على يده قوله فى الوجه الأول القرآن ظهر على يده وهو معجز . قلنا الاستقصاء في الأسئلة والأجوبة على هذا الوجه مذكور في كتاب النهاية قوله فى الوجه الثانى أشبع الخلق الكثير من الطعام القليل . - قلنا هذه الأشياء لو وجدت لنفلت إلينا نقلا متواترا لأنها أمور عجيبة والدواعي على نقلها متوفرة فلما تنقل نقلا متواترا علمنا أنها ليست صحيحة ، سلمنا سلامتها عن الطعن لكن لا نزاع في أنها لم تنقل إلينا نقلا متواترا ، بل إنما نقلت على سبيل الآحاد ورواية الآحاد لا تفيد العلم . لم. أقول : إعجاز القرآن على قول قدماء المتكلمين وبعض المحدثين فى فصاحته وعلى قول بعض المتأخرين في صرف عقول الفصحاء القادرين على المعارضة عن إيراد المعارضة قالوا كل أهل صناعة اختلفوا في تجويد تلك الصناعة فلا محالة يكون فيهم واحد لا يبلغ غيره شأوه وعجز الباقون عن معارضته ، ولا يكون ذلك معجزا له لأن ذلك لا يكون خرقا للعادة لكن صرف عقول أقرانه القادرين على معارضته يكون خرقا للعادة فذلك هو المعجز ، والاستدلال بالأخلاق والأفعال أيضا قوى وهو معنى قوله تعالى, ويتلوه شاهد منه ، وإن ذلك يشهد على صدقه في دعواه وهو صادر منه
If it is said: We do not accept that a miracle appeared at his hand. Regarding his statement in the first aspect: 'The Quran appeared at his hand and it is a miracle.' We say: The detailed discussion of questions and answers on this aspect is mentioned in the book al-Nihaya. Regarding his statement in the second aspect: 'He fed a large multitude from a small amount of food.' We say: If these things existed, they would have been transmitted to us by tawatur because they are wondrous matters and the motives for transmitting them are abundant. Since they were not transmitted by tawatur, we know they are not true. Even if we accept they are free from criticism, there is no dispute that they were not transmitted to us by tawatur; rather, they were transmitted as ahad reports, and ahad reports do not yield knowledge. ... I say: The inimitability of the Quran, according to the early mutakallimun and some later ones, lies in its eloquence, and according to some later ones, in the diversion of the minds of the eloquent capable of opposing it from producing opposition. They said: Every group of craftsmen differ in perfecting that craft, so inevitably there is one among them whose skill cannot be matched by others, and the rest are unable to oppose him, but that is not a miracle for him because that is not a breaking of custom. However, the diversion of the minds of his peers who are capable of opposing it is a breaking of custom, so that is the miracle. And the inference from character and deeds is also strong, and that is the meaning of His statement: 'and a witness from him recites it', and that witnesses to his truthfulness in his claim, and it emanates from him.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage contains objections and answers. The first objection: We do not accept that a miracle appeared at his hand. The response: the detailed refutation is in al-Nihaya. The second objection: The reported miracles like feeding many are not mutawatir, so they do not yield certainty. The answer concedes that they are ahad reports and do not give knowledge. The editor then discusses the nature of the Quran's miracle: some say it is in its eloquence (i'jaz), others say it is the sarfa (diversion of minds) from producing its like. The latter is considered a true miracle because it breaks the custom. The editor also notes that the holistic proof from character is strong, citing the verse 'and a witness from him recites it' (Hud 11:17) as supporting that. The editor's comment thus provides an important theological nuance on the nature of i'jaz.

Key Terms
تواتر
آحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahad reports are sufficient to establish matters of faith with certainty.
Correction: In classical kalam, ahad reports do not produce certain knowledge (yaqin); only mutawatir reports do.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the reported miracles like feeding the multitude not considered reliable?
A) They contradict the Quran.
B) They are not transmitted by tawatur.
C) They are not mentioned in authentic hadith.
D) They are rationally impossible.

Key Takeaway
The transmission of miracles must be mutawatir to yield certainty; ahad reports are insufficient for establishing prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a story about a miracle or supernatural event without checking how it was transmitted? How does this lesson challenge you to be more rigorous in your beliefs?
?
Think of a belief you hold that is based on a single source or a few reports. Are you willing to reassess its certainty after learning that only mutawatir reports give knowledge?
?
This lesson treats the transmission of historical reports as a matter of certainty or probability. In your daily life, which of your religious convictions are built on certain transmission, and which are merely probable? What will you do to seek certainty?

What Makes the Quran a Miracle? Eloquence vs. Sarfa
Discussion of two theories of Quranic inimitability and mention of character proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main theories of Quranic inimitability: inherent eloquence and the sarfa theory (divine prevention), and why the latter is considered a true miracle.

Core Idea
True miracle breaks natural custom; sarfa is a clear break, unlike mere eloquence.

This lesson delves into the nature of the Quran's miraculousness. Early theologians held that its unparalleled eloquence (i'jaz al-fasaaha) proved its divine origin. Later scholars proposed the sarfa theory: Allah prevented the eloquent Arabs from producing its like, which is a genuine break from natural custom. The editor also points to another proof from the Prophet's character and deeds, citing a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the editor's discussion
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'I say' (لم. أقول). Notice how the editor presents two views on Quranic inimitability.
2
Compare the two theories
Identify the first theory (eloquence) and the second (sarfa). Understand why sarfa is considered a miracle: it involves breaking custom by diverting minds.
The editor explains that mere superior eloquence is not a miracle because it is not a breach of natural law.
3
Consider the additional proof
The editor also cites the verse 'and a witness from him recites it' (Hud 11:17) as supporting the proof from character and deeds. Reflect on how this complements the Quranic miracle.
This shows that multiple lines of evidence reinforce prophethood.

فإن قيل لا نسلم أنه ظهر المعجز على يده قوله فى الوجه الأول القرآن ظهر على يده وهو معجز . قلنا الاستقصاء في الأسئلة والأجوبة على هذا الوجه مذكور في كتاب النهاية قوله فى الوجه الثانى أشبع الخلق الكثير من الطعام القليل . - قلنا هذه الأشياء لو وجدت لنفلت إلينا نقلا متواترا لأنها أمور عجيبة والدواعي على نقلها متوفرة فلما تنقل نقلا متواترا علمنا أنها ليست صحيحة ، سلمنا سلامتها عن الطعن لكن لا نزاع في أنها لم تنقل إلينا نقلا متواترا ، بل إنما نقلت على سبيل الآحاد ورواية الآحاد لا تفيد العلم . لم. أقول : إعجاز القرآن على قول قدماء المتكلمين وبعض المحدثين فى فصاحته وعلى قول بعض المتأخرين في صرف عقول الفصحاء القادرين على المعارضة عن إيراد المعارضة قالوا كل أهل صناعة اختلفوا في تجويد تلك الصناعة فلا محالة يكون فيهم واحد لا يبلغ غيره شأوه وعجز الباقون عن معارضته ، ولا يكون ذلك معجزا له لأن ذلك لا يكون خرقا للعادة لكن صرف عقول أقرانه القادرين على معارضته يكون خرقا للعادة فذلك هو المعجز ، والاستدلال بالأخلاق والأفعال أيضا قوى وهو معنى قوله تعالى, ويتلوه شاهد منه ، وإن ذلك يشهد على صدقه في دعواه وهو صادر منه
If it is said: We do not accept that a miracle appeared at his hand. Regarding his statement in the first aspect: 'The Quran appeared at his hand and it is a miracle.' We say: The detailed discussion of questions and answers on this aspect is mentioned in the book al-Nihaya. Regarding his statement in the second aspect: 'He fed a large multitude from a small amount of food.' We say: If these things existed, they would have been transmitted to us by tawatur because they are wondrous matters and the motives for transmitting them are abundant. Since they were not transmitted by tawatur, we know they are not true. Even if we accept they are free from criticism, there is no dispute that they were not transmitted to us by tawatur; rather, they were transmitted as ahad reports, and ahad reports do not yield knowledge. ... I say: The inimitability of the Quran, according to the early mutakallimun and some later ones, lies in its eloquence, and according to some later ones, in the diversion of the minds of the eloquent capable of opposing it from producing opposition. They said: Every group of craftsmen differ in perfecting that craft, so inevitably there is one among them whose skill cannot be matched by others, and the rest are unable to oppose him, but that is not a miracle for him because that is not a breaking of custom. However, the diversion of the minds of his peers who are capable of opposing it is a breaking of custom, so that is the miracle. And the inference from character and deeds is also strong, and that is the meaning of His statement: 'and a witness from him recites it', and that witnesses to his truthfulness in his claim, and it emanates from him.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage contains objections and answers. The first objection: We do not accept that a miracle appeared at his hand. The response: the detailed refutation is in al-Nihaya. The second objection: The reported miracles like feeding many are not mutawatir, so they do not yield certainty. The answer concedes that they are ahad reports and do not give knowledge. The editor then discusses the nature of the Quran's miracle: some say it is in its eloquence (i'jaz), others say it is the sarfa (diversion of minds) from producing its like. The latter is considered a true miracle because it breaks the custom. The editor also notes that the holistic proof from character is strong, citing the verse 'and a witness from him recites it' (Hud 11:17) as supporting that. The editor's comment thus provides an important theological nuance on the nature of i'jaz.

Key Terms
إعجاز
صرفة
فصاحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran's eloquence alone is sufficient to prove its miraculous origin without any divine intervention.
Correction: The passage argues that eloquence alone is not a miracle because it does not break natural custom; the sarfa theory posits a necessary divine intervention.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the sarfa theory make the Quran a true miracle?
A) Because the Quran is extremely eloquent.
B) Because Allah diverted the minds of the eloquent, breaking natural custom.
C) Because the Arabs were incapable of writing.
D) Because the Quran contains scientific facts.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is best explained by the sarfa theory, which involves divine prevention and constitutes a true break from natural custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says that mere superiority in a craft is not a miracle. In what areas of your life do you claim credit for your talents, forgetting that God could have diverted others' abilities? How would acknowledging His role change your gratitude?
?
The Prophet's character and deeds are a witness to his truthfulness. If your character were scrutinized as evidence of your faith, what would it testify? What one action can you take today to make your character a clearer witness?

Objection to the Qur'an as Miracle and Author's Response
Objection to the Qur'an as miracle and author's brief response referencing al-Nihayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that theological objections are often met with referrals to detailed discussions in other works, and that the Qur'an's miraculous nature is a key proof for prophethood.

Core Idea
An objection deferred is not an objection unanswered; the proof is found elsewhere.

In this passage, an imaginary objector challenges the first proof of prophethood by denying that the Qur'an is a miracle. The author, Imam Razi, does not repeat the full argument but directs the reader to another of his works for exhaustive treatment. This teaches us that scholars often relied on a network of texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's statement: 'We do not accept that a miracle appeared at his hand.' Understand the challenge being made.
Notice the formulaic style of objection (فإن قيل).
2
Examine the author's response
Razi simply says the exhaustive discussion is in his book al-Nihayah. Reflect on why a scholar might do this instead of repeating arguments.
This shows the interconnectedness of Islamic scholarly works.
3
Reflect on the methodology
Consider how this approach preserves space and avoids redundancy, while still acknowledging the objection.
It also demonstrates trust in the reader's ability to refer elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل لا نسلم أنه ظهر المعجز على يده قوله فى الوجه الأول القرآن ظهر على يده وهو معجز . قلنا الاستقصاء في الأسئلة والأجوبة على هذا الوجه مذكور في كتاب النهاية (۱) أقول : إعجاز القرآن على قول قدماء المتكلمين وبعض المحدثين فى فصاحته وعلى قول بعض المتأخرين في صرف عقول الفصحاء القادرين على المعارضة عن إيراد المعارضة قالوا كل أهل صناعة اختلفوا في تجويد تلك الصناعة فلا محالة يكون فيهم واحد لا يبلغ غيره شأوه وعجز الباقون عن معارضته ، ولا يكون ذلك معجزا له لأن ذلك لا يكون خرقا للعادة لكن صرف عقول أقرانه القادرين على معارضته يكون خرقا للعادة فذلك هو المعجز ، والاستدلال بالأخلاق والأفعال أيضا قوى وهو معنى قوله تعالى, ويتلوه شاهد منه ، وإن ذلك يشهد على صدقه في دعواه وهو صادر منه
If it is said: We do not accept that a miracle appeared at his hand. His statement in the first aspect: The Qur'an appeared at his hand and it is miraculous. We say: Exhaustiveness in the questions and answers on this aspect is mentioned in the book 'al-Nihāyah'. (1) I say: The inimitability (i'jāz) of the Qur'an according to the early theologians and some later scholars is in its eloquence (faṣāḥa), and according to some later scholars, it is in diverting the minds of eloquent persons capable of opposing it from producing opposition. However, diverting the minds of his peers who are capable of opposing it is a breaking of custom, so that is the miracle. The inference from morals and actions is also strong, and that is the meaning of His statement: 'and a witness from him recites it' (Qur'an 11:17), and that testifies to his truth in his claim, and it originates from him.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage records an objection to the first proof (the Qur'an as miracle) and the author's brief response that the detailed discussion is in another book. The editor then adds a note explaining the two main theories of i'jāz: the early view that it lies in the Qur'an's eloquence, and the later view that it lies in God turning the minds of the eloquent Arabs away from producing a rival text. The latter is considered a breaking of custom (kharq al-'āda) and thus a true miracle. The editor also links this to the inference from the Prophet's character.

Correction: The author is referring to a more detailed treatment in another work, not conceding the point.

The author's reference to another book shows the interconnectedness of theological works and the efficiency of scholarly discourse.
