First objection: miracles not necessarily for authentication
Razi raises the objection that miracles may not be created for authentication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first objection to the miracle-based proof of prophethood: that miracles may not necessarily be created for authentication, and why this challenges the standard argument.

Core Idea
Miracles may not be for authentication; God's acts are not purposive.

In this passage, Razi begins the second station of his critique of proofs for prophethood. He presents the first of two objections: the claim that miracles are not necessarily created for the purpose of authentication. He argues that if God's acts are not driven by purposes, then we cannot be certain that the miracle was intended to confirm the prophet's truthfulness. This objection strikes at the heart of the common argument from miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the English translation. Notice how Razi sets up his objection using a dilemma.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the dilemma
The objection presents two possibilities about acts without a motive: either they are permissible or not. Understand each branch.
3
Trace the consequences
Follow each branch to see how it undermines the certainty of miracles for authentication.
The second branch leads to attributing evil to God.
4
Connect to the larger context
This objection challenges the assumption that miracles are clear signs of truthfulness. Reflect on why this matters for the proof of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني ففيه النزاع من وجهين . أحدهما : لا نسلم خلق المعجز لأجل التصديق لاسيما وقد بينتم أن أفعال الله تعالى لا يجوز أن تكون معللة بالغرض ، وبما يحقق هذا أن الفعل بدون الداعى إما أن يكون جائزا أو لا يكون فإن كان جائزا لم يمسكن القطع بأن الله تعالى فعل المعجز لأجل التصديق بل لعله فعله لا لأمر أصلا، وإن لم يجز توقف فعلنا للقبائح على داع يخلقه الله تعالى فيكون الله تعالى فاعلا لما يوجب القبيح ، فإذا جاز ذلك من الله تعالى فلم لا يجوز من أفضل عباده ، وإذا جاز ذلك منه بطل أصل الاستدلال بالمعجز على التصديق .
As for the second station, there is dispute in two aspects. The first: we do not concede that the miracle was created for authentication, especially since you have stated that the acts of God, exalted, cannot be caused by an objective. And what confirms this is that an act without a motive is either permissible or not. If it is permissible, then we cannot be certain that God did the miracle for authentication; rather, perhaps He did it for no reason at all. And if it is not permissible (for an act to occur without a motive), then our performance of evil deeds depends on a motive that God creates, so God would be the doer of what leads to evil. So if that is permissible from God, why is it not permissible from His most virtuous servants? And if that is permissible from Him, then the whole basis of using the miracle as proof of authentication is invalidated.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage begins the second station (المقام الثاني) which contains two objections to the miracle-based proof of prophethood. The first objection denies that miracles are created specifically for authentication (التصديق). The argument relies on the premise that God's acts are not purposive (معللة بالغرض). If an act can occur without a motive, then we cannot be certain the miracle was for authentication; it might have no purpose at all. If an act cannot occur without a motive, then our evil deeds depend on a motive created by God, making God the cause of evil (which Razi's opponents allow as possible from God). If that is permissible from God, then it is also permissible from the best of His servants (the prophet), which would undermine the incentive to obey him. Hence, the argument for authentication fails.

Key Terms
المقام الثاني (second station)
التصديق (authentication)
الغرض (purpose)
الداعي (motive)
القبيح (evil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are always created specifically to authenticate the prophet.
Correction: Razi argues that God's acts are not purposive, so miracles may not be for authentication; they could be purposeless or for other reasons.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the first objection in the second station?
A) Miracles are always for authentication
B) We do not concede that miracles are created for authentication
C) God cannot do miracles
D) The prophet is not truthful

Key Takeaway
Razi's first objection argues that miracles may not be created for authentication, using a dilemma about motives that undermines the certainty of the miracle-based proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's dilemma asks you to consider that a miracle might be done for no reason at all. If you cannot be certain that a miracle has a purpose, what does that do to your trust in the signs of prophethood? Is your belief in the Prophet based on rational proof or on something deeper that withstands such doubt?
?
One horn of the dilemma leads to God being the cause of evil. When you consider that, does your concept of God's justice and goodness feel challenged? How do you reconcile this with your personal understanding of God's relationship to evil in your own life?
?
If the purpose of a miracle is not given, then your acceptance of prophethood must rest on something other than external proof. What is that foundation for you personally, and is it strong enough to survive this critique?

Five Alternative Purposes for a Miracle
Razi lists five alternative purposes for a miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five alternative purposes for which a miracle might be performed according to Razi's objection.

Core Idea
A miracle can have many purposes beyond authentication.

Razi grants that miracles have a purpose but challenges the claim that authentication is the only one. He demands proof of exclusivity and then generously offers five alternative divine purposes, showing that the miracle's purpose is not necessarily to prove prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its literal translation to get the overall flow. Notice the concessive opening and the list of five purposes.
Razi uses 'على سبيل التبرع' to indicate he is offering these alternatives as a favor.
2
Identify the concessive start
Razi concedes that the miracle has a purpose but asks why it must be exclusively authentication. Find this in the passage.
The opponent must prove the exclusivity (الحصر).
3
Examine each of the five purposes
Go through each of the five alternatives: initiating a custom, repeating a long-standing custom, honoring a saint or another prophet, being a precursor, and testing minds.
Notice the example of the eighth sphere's cycle for the second purpose.
4
Reflect on the argumentative force
Consider how these alternatives weaken the opponent's position. If miracles can have other purposes, then the mere occurrence of a miracle does not prove prophethood.
This objection is part of a larger dialectical exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانى : سلمنا أنه تعالى فعل المعجز لمقصود لكن لم قلت إن ذلك المقصود ليس إلا التصديق ولعله تعالى فعله لغرض آخر وعليكم بيان الحصر، ثم إنا على سبيل التبرع نذكر أمورا أخر . أحدها: أن يفعله ليكون ابتداء عادة . وثانيها : ليكون تكرير العادة متطاولة فإن الفلك الثامن لا يستكمل الدورة إلا في ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة فيكون وصولها إلى أول الحمل في مثل هذه المدة عادة لها ، فلعل هذا الحادث يكون من هذا الباب . وثالتها : أن يكون ذلك كرامة لولى أو معجزة لتى آخر في طرف آخر من أطراف العالم . ورابعها : أن يكون ذلك ارهاصا لتى آخر يأتي بعد ذلك كالاحوال التي ظهرت على محمد قبل بعثته وكالنور الذى يحكى أنه كان يظهر فى جبين أبيه . وخامسها : أن يكون امتحانا لعقول المكلفين كما أنزل المتشابهات امتحانا لمقولهم .
Second: We grant that He, exalted, did the miracle for an intended purpose, but why do you say that purpose is nothing other than authentication? Perhaps He did it for another purpose, and you must provide the proof of exclusivity. Then, as a favor, we mention other matters. First: that He does it to initiate a custom. Second: that it be a repetition of a long-standing custom, for the eighth sphere completes its cycle only in thirty-six thousand years, so its arrival at the beginning of Aries in such a period becomes a custom; perhaps this event is of that kind. Third: that it be a miracle of honor for a saint or a miracle for another prophet at another part of the world. Fourth: that it be a precursor for a later prophet, like the conditions that appeared before Muhammad's mission and the light that is reported to have appeared on his father's forehead. Fifth: that it be a test for the minds of the obligated, just as ambiguous verses were sent down as a test for their intellects.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is the second objection of the second station. It concedes that the miracle has a purpose but denies that it must be authentication exclusively. Razi asks the opponent to demonstrate exclusivity. Then he generously (على سبيل التبرع) offers five alternative purposes: (1) to establish a new custom (عادة) in the universe; (2) to repeat a long-term custom, citing the eighth sphere's 36,000-year cycle; (3) to honor a saint (ولی) or be a miracle for another prophet elsewhere; (4) to be a precursor (ارهاص) for a later prophet, like events before Muhammad's prophethood or the light on Abdullah's forehead; (5) to test the minds of the responsible beings (المكلفين), similar to ambiguous Quranic verses. These alternatives show that the miracle's purpose is not necessarily authentication, weakening the opponent's argument.

Key Terms
القصد (intention)
الحصر (exclusivity)
العادة (custom)
الفلك الثامن (eighth sphere)
الكرامة (miracle of honor)
الولي (saint)
الإرهاص (precursor)
الامتحان (test)
المتشابهات (ambiguous verses)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A miracle's only purpose is to authenticate a prophet's claim.
Correction: Razi shows that a miracle may serve other divine purposes, such as establishing a custom, honoring a saint, or testing minds.

Checkpoint
How many alternative purposes for a miracle does Razi list?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Razi's objection shows that the purpose of a miracle is not limited to authentication; it can serve various divine intentions, undermining the opponent's argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi suggests that some miracles might be tests for the mind (imtihan). Are there events in your life that you have dismissed as random or meaningless, that could actually be a divine test of your intellect and faith? How would you respond differently if you saw them as such?
?
The passage includes the possibility that a miracle could be a 'karamah' for a saint—not just a proof for a prophet. Have you ever witnessed something extraordinary in the life of a righteous person and assumed it was mere coincidence? Will you now pause to consider it might be a sign from Allah to draw you closer to Him?
?
Razi shifts the burden of proof to the opponent: why must a miracle's purpose be only authentication? In your own relationship with God, do you limit His actions to a single purpose (e.g., punishment or reward), or do you allow for multiple, deeper wisdom? What would change in your trust and submission if you embraced the possibility that every divine act has layers of intention?

The Objection That God Could Authenticate a Liar
Razi presents an objection that divine authentication need not imply truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that even if God authenticates someone, it does not prove they are truthful, especially within the Ash'ari worldview.

Core Idea
God could authenticate a liar as He creates disbelief and indecency.

Razi presents a crucial objection to the miracle-based proof of prophethood. He first grants that God has authenticated the claimant, but then questions the inference that this necessarily means the claimant is truthful. This objection is rooted in the Ash'ari understanding that God creates all actions, including evil, so authenticating a liar is not impossible for Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening of the third station
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the conditional acceptance
Note that Razi begins by granting that God authenticated the claimant. This sets the stage for the objection.
3
Understand the Ash'ari premise
Razi mentions that God creates disbelief and indecencies. In Ash'ari theology, God's acts are not subject to human moral categories.
This is key to the objection.
4
Recognize the scope of the objection
Razi notes that this objection is specific to Ash'aris, not Mu'tazila, who deny God creates evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثالث : سلمنا أن الله تعالى صدقه لكن فلم قلتم إن كل من صدقه الله فهو صادق فإن عندكم اسمه تعالى خلق الكفر والفواحش فإذا لم يقبح ذلك من الله تعالى فلم لا يحسن منه أيضاً تصديق الكاذب ، وهذا السؤال الأخير مختص بنا دون المعتزلة . ثم نقول : هب أنا لا نذكر شيئاً من هذه الاحتمالات فلم قلت إن كل من ظهر عليه المعجز كان رسولا والرجوع فيه إلى المثال ضعيف لأنا لا نقطع في ذلك المثال يصدق المدعى ، لأنه ربما قام الملك العظيم في ذلك الوقت لحدوث ألم في بطنه أو شاهد شيئا فخاف أو تذكر أمرا فقام طلباله . وبالجملة فليس هنا إلا الدوران ، وهو أنه قام عند التماس المدعى ، وما قام قبل ذلك، والدوران لا يفيد إلا الظن الضعيف ، فإنه يحكى أن واحدا كان يجلس في مسجد فكلما دخل المؤذن قام ذلك الإنسان وخرج ، فقال له المؤذن مالى أراك كلما أذنت خرجت فقال لابل كلما هممت بالخروج أذنت، وهذا يدل على أن دلالة الدوران على العلمية ضعيف، ثم إن سلمنا دلالة العقل على التصديق فلم قلت إنه فى حق الله تعالى كذلك وستعرف أن القياس المؤيد بالجامع لا يفيد إلا الظن فكيف هذا القياس الخالى عن الجامع، فهذا هو الاعتراض على الدليل. الأول على النبوة .
The third station: We grant that God, exalted, authenticated him, but why do you say that everyone whom God authenticates is truthful? For according to you, He, exalted, creates disbelief and indecencies; so if that is not considered evil from God, then why is it not also good from Him to authenticate a liar? This last question is specific to us (Ash'aris), not to the Mu'tazila. Then we say: Suppose we do not mention any of these possibilities; still, why do you say that everyone upon whom a miracle appears is a messenger? And the reference to the example is weak because we are not certain in that example that the claimant is truthful, for perhaps the great king stood up at that time due to a stomach ache, or witnessed something that frightened him, or remembered something and stood up involuntarily. In short, there is nothing here but correlation: that he stood when the claimant asked, and did not stand before. Correlation only yields weak conjecture. It is related that a man was sitting in a mosque; whenever the muezzin entered, the man stood and left. The muezzin said, 'Why do I see that whenever I call the adhan you leave?' The man replied, 'Rather, whenever I intend to leave, you call the adhan.' This shows that the indication of correlation for certainty is weak. And if we grant that reason indicates authentication, why do you say it is so with respect to God? And you will know that a syllogism supported by a common property (al-qiyas al-mu'ayyad bi'l-jami') only gives conjecture, so what about this syllogism lacking a common property? This is the objection to the first proof of prophethood.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is the third station (المقام الثالث) of objections against the miracle-based proof. First, it argues that even if God authenticates someone, it does not entail that person is truthful, because in the Ash'ari school (which holds that God creates all acts, including evil), God could authenticate a liar just as He creates disbelief. This objection is specific to Ash'aris, not Mu'tazila (who deny God creates evil). Then Razi adds further: even ignoring all previous possibilities, why assume that anyone who performs a miracle is a prophet? The reference to an example (perhaps a king standing at a claimant's request) is weak because the king might have stood for another reason. Thus, the only evidence is correlation (دوران) between the claimant's request and the event, which yields only weak conjecture. Razi illustrates with a story: a man in a mosque always leaves when the muezzin calls the adhan, but the causality could be reversed. So correlation does not prove causation. Finally, even if reason indicated authentication, why apply that same reasoning to God? A syllogism with a shared property (قياس مؤيد بالجامع) is only conjectural, and here there is no common property. This concludes the objections to the first proof of prophethood.

Key Terms
المقام الثالث
التصديق
الكذب
الفحش
الكفر
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God authenticates someone, that person must be truthful.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, God can authenticate a liar because He is not constrained by human notions of good and evil.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why could God authenticate a liar?
A) Because God cannot lie
B) Because God creates all actions including evil
C) Because the Mu'tazila allow it
D) Because authentication is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Divine authentication does not necessarily prove truthfulness, especially within the Ash'ari framework.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself judging God's actions based on your own sense of what is fair? In what area of your life do you struggle to accept that God's decree is not subject to human moral reasoning? What would it take for you to surrender that judgment completely?
?
If you believed that God could authenticate a liar, would that shake your trust in the truthfulness of the Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ)? Does your certainty rest on rational arguments like miracles, or on something deeper in your heart? What would you need to strengthen to remain steadfast even if your rational proof were questioned?
?
When you witness terrible evil in the world—oppression, suffering, disbelief—do you ever feel troubled about God's justice? How does the Ash'ari position that God is not accountable to human standards of good and evil free you from that burden? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived by that conviction?

The Weakness of Correlation and Syllogism in Proving Prophethood
Razi critiques the inference from miracles using the weakness of correlation and syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of using correlation and syllogism to prove prophethood, and why they only yield weak conjecture.

Core Idea
Correlation does not prove causation, and syllogisms without a common property are weak.

After raising the possibility that God could authenticate a liar, Razi goes further to criticize the very method of inferring prophethood from miracles. He argues that even if we ignore all previous objections, the inference based on correlation is weak, as illustrated by a story of a man and a muezzin. He then points out that the reasoning used is a syllogism without a common property, which only gives conjecture.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'ثم نقول' to the end. This part sets aside earlier objections and focuses on the inference from miracles.
2
Understand the weakness of the example
Razi says the example of the king standing is uncertain because he might have stood for other reasons.
This shows the danger of assuming causality.
3
Analyze the concept of correlation
Razi explains that only correlation is observed, which yields only weak conjecture.
The story of the man and muezzin illustrates the fallacy.
4
Recognize the critique of the syllogism
Razi argues that even if inference were valid, applying it to God is problematic; the syllogism lacks a common property.
This concludes the objection to the first proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then we say: Suppose we do not mention any of these possibilities; still, why do you say that everyone upon whom a miracle appears is a messenger? The man replied, 'Rather, whenever I intend to leave, you call the adhan.' This shows that the indication of correlation for certainty is weak. And if we grant that reason indicates authentication, why do you say it is so with respect to God? And you will know that a syllogism supported by a common property (al-qiyas al-mu'ayyad bi'l-jami') only gives conjecture, so what about this syllogism lacking a common property? Commentary
This passage is the third station (المقام الثالث) of objections against the miracle-based proof. Second Proof: Moral Character Does Not Prove Prophethood
Razi argues that good moral character does not prove prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi considers the argument from moral character insufficient to prove prophethood.

Core Idea
Moral excellence does not uniquely identify prophethood.

Imam Razi continues his critique of proofs for prophethood by addressing the argument from moral character. He argues that while prophets are indeed virtuous, virtue alone is not sufficient proof of a divine mission, as many non-prophets have also displayed remarkable ethical conduct.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic carefully. Notice Razi's strong opening: 'الدليل الثانى ... فضعيف'. This sets the tone for his critique.
The word 'ضعيف' (weak) is a clear dismissal.
2
Identify Razi's counterargument
Razi concedes that moral character shows distinction but asks: 'وكيف وقد حكى عن أفاضل الحكماء...' – he points to virtuous philosophers as counterexamples.
Philosophers (الحكماء) also had excellent ethics and knowledge.
3
Understand the logical flaw
The core point: moral excellence is not exclusive to prophets. Therefore, it cannot serve as a unique proof of prophethood.
This refutes the claim that good character alone establishes prophecy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثانى وهو الاستدلال بمحاسن أحواله على نبوته فضعيف ، لأن غاية ما في الباب أنه يدل على كون ذلك الإنسان متميزا عن سائر الناس بمزيد الفضيلة ، ولكن من أين يدل على النبوة وكيف وقد حكى عن أفاضل الحكماء في الأخلاق أمور عجيبة جعلها الناس قدوة لأنفسهم في الدنيا والآخرة مع ما نقل عنهم من العلوم الدقيقة .
The second proof – namely, inferring his prophethood from the excellence of his states – is weak. For the utmost it indicates is that that person is distinguished from other people by an excess of virtue. But how does it indicate prophethood? And indeed, wonderful things have been reported from the most virtuous of philosophers regarding ethics, which people have taken as examples for themselves in this world and the next, along with what has been transmitted from them of precise sciences.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage dismisses the second proof of prophethood, which appeals to the prophet's excellent moral character and conduct (محاسن أحواله). Razi argues that this only shows the person is exceptionally virtuous, not that he is a prophet. Many philosophers (الحكماء) were also reported to have amazing ethical qualities and were taken as role models, and they also possessed deep knowledge. Therefore, moral excellence does not uniquely indicate prophethood.

Key Terms
المحاسن (excellences)
الأحوال (states)
الفضيلة (virtue)
الحكماء (philosophers)
العلوم الدقيقة (precise sciences)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moral character alone is sufficient evidence that someone is a prophet.
Correction: Razi shows that virtuous philosophers also had excellent character and knowledge, so moral excellence does not uniquely indicate prophethood.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider the second proof (moral character) weak?
A) Because prophets have bad character too
B) Because virtuous philosophers also existed
C) Because moral character is not observable
D) Because the proof relies on correlation

Key Takeaway
Good moral character, however admirable, does not necessarily indicate prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi used philosophers as a counterexample. When you learn about non-Muslims with high moral standards, do you feel a tension? How does this lesson challenge you to separate admiration for virtue from allegiance to truth? What would it take for you to prioritize the latter?

Objection to Scriptural Prophecies
Razi's objection to scriptural prophecies as proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's dialectical objection to using scriptural prophecies as proof of Muhammad's prophethood.

Core Idea
Scriptural prophecies are either too detailed (false) or too general (ambiguous).

Razi presents the third proof and immediately raises a powerful objection. He forces the defender to choose: either the prophecies are detailed or general. Both options lead to problems. This lesson dissects that argument and shows how Razi challenges the reliability of scriptural prophecies as a proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options
Read the passage and note the two possibilities: detailed prophecies (with time, place, description) or general prophecies (without specification).
Razi sets up a dilemma to trap the opponent.
2
Understand why detailed is false
Razi claims that the current Torah and Gospel are devoid of such detailed descriptions. He anticipates the objection of alteration and rebuts it by arguing that widely known books cannot be fully altered.
3
Understand why general is insufficient
Even if general prophecies exist, they only indicate a virtuous person, not necessarily Muhammad. They could refer to someone else.
The proof fails because it does not conclusively identify Muhammad.
4
Reflect on the alteration argument
Razi rejects the claim that Jews and Christians altered the books, saying such widespread books are immune to complete alteration, similar to the Qur'an.
This is a noteworthy point about textual integrity.
5
Connect to the broader debate
Consider how this objection fits into the overall proof structure. Razi leaves the objection standing, but the footnote later responds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدليل الثالث وهو الاستدلال بما جاء فى التوراة والإنجيل على نبوة محمد الله فالاعتراض عليه أنكم إما أن تقولوا أنه جاء في هذه الكتب صفة محمد على الله على سبيل التفصيل بمعنى أنه تعالى بين أنه سيجيء في السنة القابلية في البلدة العلانية شخص وصفه كذا وكذا فاعلموا أنه رسولى ، وأما أن لا يقولوا كذلك بل يقولوا إنه تعالى بين ذلك بيانا مجملا من غير تعيين الزمان والمكان والوصف ، فإن ادعيتم لأول فهو باطل ، لانا نج. التوراة والإنجيل خالية عنه. لا يقال اليهود والنصارى حرفوا هذين الكتابين ، لانا نقول هذان كتابان مشهوران في المشارق والمغارب ومثل هذا مما لا يصح تطرق التحريف إليه كما في القرآن ، وإن ادعيتم الثاني فبتقدير المساعدة عليه لا يدل على النبوة ، بل ربما دل على ظهور إنسان فاضل شريف ، أو إن دل على إذ ربما كان المبشر به إنسانا آخر (۱) النبوة لكن لا يدل على نبوة محمد . (۱) أقول : هذا الذي ذكره كله بمنزلة شبه السوفسطائية فإن التمين الحاصل للعقلاء إذا قام إنسان على طريق مرضية عند الخواص والعوام وادعى أنه مبعوث من عند الله ، والدليل على صدق قولى أن الله تعالى يظهر على يدى أمرا خارقا للعادة فظهر ، وقال من لم يصدقنى بمثل ما ظهر على يدى وتحدى من عداه لا يزول بأمثال هذه الاحتمالات وقد أشار المصنف أيضاً إلى هذا المعنى في الجواب على ما سيأتى . وأما المذكور فى التوراة والإنجيل الداله على نبوته الا الله وعلى آله وصحبه فكثيرة يذكرها المصنفون من الواقفين عليها منها ما ذكر فى التوراة بعبارة تفسيرها هكذا جاء الرب من طور سينا وظهر بساعير وعلا بفاران ، وفى التوراة أن إسماعيل كان فى سرية فاران يعنى بادية العرب وذكر الواقفون على جبالها أن فاران فى طريق مكة قبل الدون بميلين ونصف وهو كان المنزل المسافرين على يسار الطريق من العراق إلى مكة ، ومنه ما جاء في السفر الخامس أن الرب قال لموسى « إنى مقهم لهم نبيا مثلك من بنى أخوتهم وأيما رجل لم يسمع كلماتي التي يؤديها من ذلك الرجل باسمى أنا أنتقم منه ، ومنها في السفر الأول لهاجر أنها تلد ويكون من ولدها من يده فوق الجميع ويد الجميع مبسوطة إلى بالخشوع، وأيضاً جاء فى الإنجيل فى الفصل الرابع عشر في إنجيل يوحنا أن المسيح قال إنى أسأل أبى أن يعطيكم فارقليطا آخر يكون معكم إلى الأبد روح الحق ، والفارقليط معناه كاشف الخفيات، وأمثال هذا في هذين الكتابين وفى كتب سائر الأنبياء التى عندهم كثير يطول الكتاب بذكرها ولا يقدر المخالف على دفعها أو صرفها إلى ملك أو نبي آخر ولا على أن يكتمها .
As for the third proof – namely, the inference from what came in the Torah and Gospel to the prophethood of Muhammad, may God bless him – the objection against it is that you either say that in these books the description of Muhammad is given in detail, meaning that He, exalted, made clear that in the coming year in a certain town a person of such-and-such description will come, so know that he is My messenger; or you do not say that, but say that He, exalted, made it clear in a general way without specifying time, place, or description. If you claim the first, it is false, for we find the Torah and Gospel devoid of it. It cannot be said that the Jews and Christians altered these two books, for we say these are two books famous in the East and West, and such a thing is not susceptible to alteration like the Qur'an. And if you claim the second, even if granted, it does not indicate prophethood; perhaps it indicates the appearance of a virtuous, noble person, or if it does indicate, then perhaps the one foretold is someone else – it indicates prophethood but not the prophethood of Muhammad. (Footnote): I say: All that he mentioned is like sophistical doubts, for the confidence attained by rational people, when a person rises upon a path pleasing to the elite and commoners and claims to be sent from God, and the proof of the truth of my statement is that God manifests a supernatural act through me, and it appears, and he says, 'Let those who do not believe me produce the like of what appeared through me,' and challenges others, is not removed by such possibilities. The author (Razi) himself has pointed to this meaning in the answer later. As for what is mentioned in the Torah and Gospel indicating his prophethood – may God bless him and his family and companions – there are many, mentioned by authors who have studied them. Among them is what is mentioned in the Torah in interpretation: 'The Lord came from Sinai, appeared at Seir, and shone forth from Paran.' And in the Torah, Ishmael was in the wilderness of Paran, meaning the desert of the Arabs, and those who know its mountains say Paran is on the road to Mecca, two and a half miles before the valley, and it was a lodging for travelers on the left side of the road from Iraq to Mecca. Another is in the fifth book: the Lord said to Moses, 'I will raise up for them a prophet like you from among their brothers; and whoever does not listen to my words that he speaks in my name, I will require it of him.' Another is in the first book about Hagar: she will give birth, and from her child, his hand will be over all, and the hand of all will be stretched out to him in humility. Also in the Gospel, in the Gospel of John, chapter fourteen, the Messiah said, 'I will ask the Father, and He will give you another Paraclete to be with you forever, the Spirit of truth.' The Paraclete means 'revealer of hidden things.' And similar statements in these two books and the books of the other prophets that they have are many; the book would be lengthened by mentioning them, and the opponent cannot refute them or divert them to another king or prophet, nor can he conceal them.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage covers the third proof of prophethood: scriptural prophecies in the Torah and Gospel about Muhammad. Razi first presents an objection: either the prophecies are detailed (specifying time, place, description) or general. If detailed, they are false because the current Torah and Gospel do not contain such details. The objection anticipates that Jews and Christians could have altered the texts, but Razi counters that these are widely known books and alteration is impossible like the Qur'an. If general, even if accepted, they only indicate a virtuous person, not necessarily Muhammad; they could refer to someone else. This objection is left standing. Then a lengthy footnote (starting with 'أقول') defends the miracle proof and provides specific scriptural examples from Torah and Gospel that point to Muhammad. The footnote argues that the earlier objections are sophistical, and that the miracle challenge is enough. It then lists several prophecies: the Lord coming from Sinai, Seir, and Paran (Paran being Mecca); the prophecy in Deuteronomy about a prophet like Moses from among their brothers; the prophecy about Ishmael's offspring; and the Paraclete in John. The footnote asserts these cannot be denied or diverted to another.

Key Terms
البشارات
التفصيل
الإجمال
التحريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi is rejecting the validity of all scriptural prophecies, but he is only objecting to this specific proof method.
Correction: Razi's objection is dialectical: he shows that the proof from scriptures is inconclusive, not that the scriptures are false.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is the claim that detailed prophecies exist in the Torah and Gospel false?
A) The Torah and Gospel have been completely corrupted.
B) The Torah and Gospel are devoid of such detailed descriptions.
C) The prophecies are only in the Quran.
D) Jews and Christians hide the prophecies.

Key Takeaway
Razi's objection forces a choice: detailed prophecies are absent, general prophecies are ambiguous, so the proof fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that a proof can fail if it relies on ambiguous or unverifiable details. Think of a belief you hold strongly: is it based on clear, specific evidence, or on general impressions that could apply to something else? Are you willing to examine it as rigorously as Razi examines this proof?
?
Razi dismisses the argument that the scriptures were altered because they are too widely known. In your own life, are there widely known truths that you conveniently ignore or explain away because they challenge you? Can you think of one such truth right now, and will you commit to facing it?
?
The objection here is that a general prophecy could be misapplied to the wrong person. Have you ever assumed that a general blessing or sign from God was specifically for you, only to later realize it was not? How can you guard against such spiritual arrogance in the future?

Defense of Scriptural Prophecies: The Footnote
Footnote defense of scriptural prophecies with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the footnote's defense of scriptural prophecies and specific examples from the Torah and Gospel that point to Muhammad.

Core Idea
Scriptural prophecies about Muhammad are numerous and cannot be denied.

After Razi's objection, a lengthy footnote responds, dismissing the objection as sophistical and providing concrete examples from the Torah and Gospel that point to Muhammad. This lesson explores those prophecies and the footnote's argument that the real proof is the miracle challenge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the footnote's opening dismissal
The footnote begins by calling Razi's objections sophistical. It emphasizes that the real proof is the miracle challenge, which gives confidence to rational people.
2
Identify the first prophecy: Paran
The footnote cites a verse: 'The Lord came from Sinai, appeared at Seir, and shone forth from Paran.' It identifies Paran as near Mecca.
Paran is associated with Ishmael and the wilderness of the Arabs.
3
Identify the second prophecy: Deuteronomy
God tells Moses: 'I will raise up for them a prophet like you from among their brothers.' This is seen as referring to Muhammad, who is from the brothers of the Israelites (Ishmaelites).
4
Identify the third prophecy: Ishmael's descendants
The footnote references the prophecy about Hagar: her child will have dominion over all, and all hands will be extended to him in humility.
5
Identify the fourth prophecy: Paraclete in John
Jesus promises a Paraclete, meaning 'revealer of hidden things.' The footnote argues this refers to Muhammad.
The term Paraclete is explained as 'كاشف الخفيات'.

وأما الدليل الثالث وهو الاستدلال بما جاء فى التوراة والإنجيل على نبوة محمد الله فالاعتراض عليه أنكم إما أن تقولوا أنه جاء في هذه الكتب صفة محمد على الله على سبيل التفصيل بمعنى أنه تعالى بين أنه سيجيء في السنة القابلية في البلدة العلانية شخص وصفه كذا وكذا فاعلموا أنه رسولى ، وأما أن لا يقولوا كذلك بل يقولوا إنه تعالى بين ذلك بيانا مجملا من غير تعيين الزمان والمكان والوصف ، فإن ادعيتم لأول فهو باطل ، لانا نج. التوراة والإنجيل خالية عنه. لا يقال اليهود والنصارى حرفوا هذين الكتابين ، لانا نقول هذان كتابان مشهوران في المشارق والمغارب ومثل هذا مما لا يصح تطرق التحريف إليه كما في القرآن ، وإن ادعيتم الثاني فبتقدير المساعدة عليه لا يدل على النبوة ، بل ربما دل على ظهور إنسان فاضل شريف ، أو إن دل على إذ ربما كان المبشر به إنسانا آخر (۱) النبوة لكن لا يدل على نبوة محمد . (۱) أقول : هذا الذي ذكره كله بمنزلة شبه السوفسطائية فإن التمين الحاصل للعقلاء إذا قام إنسان على طريق مرضية عند الخواص والعوام وادعى أنه مبعوث من عند الله ، والدليل على صدق قولى أن الله تعالى يظهر على يدى أمرا خارقا للعادة فظهر ، وقال من لم يصدقنى بمثل ما ظهر على يدى وتحدى من عداه لا يزول بأمثال هذه الاحتمالات وقد أشار المصنف أيضاً إلى هذا المعنى في الجواب على ما سيأتى . وأما المذكور فى التوراة والإنجيل الداله على نبوته الا الله وعلى آله وصحبه فكثيرة يذكرها المصنفون من الواقفين عليها منها ما ذكر فى التوراة بعبارة تفسيرها هكذا جاء الرب من طور سينا وظهر بساعير وعلا بفاران ، وفى التوراة أن إسماعيل كان فى سرية فاران يعنى بادية العرب وذكر الواقفون على جبالها أن فاران فى طريق مكة قبل الدون بميلين ونصف وهو كان المنزل المسافرين على يسار الطريق من العراق إلى مكة ، ومنه ما جاء في السفر الخامس أن الرب قال لموسى « إنى مقهم لهم نبيا مثلك من بنى أخوتهم وأيما رجل لم يسمع كلماتي التي يؤديها من ذلك الرجل باسمى أنا أنتقم منه ، ومنها في السفر الأول لهاجر أنها تلد ويكون من ولدها من يده فوق الجميع ويد الجميع مبسوطة إلى بالخشوع، وأيضاً جاء فى الإنجيل فى الفصل الرابع عشر في إنجيل يوحنا أن المسيح قال إنى أسأل أبى أن يعطيكم فارقليطا آخر يكون معكم إلى الأبد روح الحق ، والفارقليط معناه كاشف الخفيات، وأمثال هذا في هذين الكتابين وفى كتب سائر الأنبياء التى عندهم كثير يطول الكتاب بذكرها ولا يقدر المخالف على دفعها أو صرفها إلى ملك أو نبي آخر ولا على أن يكتمها .
As for the third proof – namely, the inference from what came in the Torah and Gospel to the prophethood of Muhammad, may God bless him – the objection against it is that you either say that in these books the description of Muhammad is given in detail, meaning that He, exalted, made clear that in the coming year in a certain town a person of such-and-such description will come, so know that he is My messenger; or you do not say that, but say that He, exalted, made it clear in a general way without specifying time, place, or description. If you claim the first, it is false, for we find the Torah and Gospel devoid of it. It cannot be said that the Jews and Christians altered these two books, for we say these are two books famous in the East and West, and such a thing is not susceptible to alteration like the Qur'an. And if you claim the second, even if granted, it does not indicate prophethood; perhaps it indicates the appearance of a virtuous, noble person, or if it does indicate, then perhaps the one foretold is someone else – it indicates prophethood but not the prophethood of Muhammad. (Footnote): I say: All that he mentioned is like sophistical doubts, for the confidence attained by rational people, when a person rises upon a path pleasing to the elite and commoners and claims to be sent from God, and the proof of the truth of my statement is that God manifests a supernatural act through me, and it appears, and he says, 'Let those who do not believe me produce the like of what appeared through me,' and challenges others, is not removed by such possibilities. The author (Razi) himself has pointed to this meaning in the answer later. As for what is mentioned in the Torah and Gospel indicating his prophethood – may God bless him and his family and companions – there are many, mentioned by authors who have studied them. Among them is what is mentioned in the Torah in interpretation: 'The Lord came from Sinai, appeared at Seir, and shone forth from Paran.' And in the Torah, Ishmael was in the wilderness of Paran, meaning the desert of the Arabs, and those who know its mountains say Paran is on the road to Mecca, two and a half miles before the valley, and it was a lodging for travelers on the left side of the road from Iraq to Mecca. Another is in the fifth book: the Lord said to Moses, 'I will raise up for them a prophet like you from among their brothers; and whoever does not listen to my words that he speaks in my name, I will require it of him.' Another is in the first book about Hagar: she will give birth, and from her child, his hand will be over all, and the hand of all will be stretched out to him in humility. Also in the Gospel, in the Gospel of John, chapter fourteen, the Messiah said, 'I will ask the Father, and He will give you another Paraclete to be with you forever, the Spirit of truth.' The Paraclete means 'revealer of hidden things.' And similar statements in these two books and the books of the other prophets that they have are many; the book would be lengthened by mentioning them, and the opponent cannot refute them or divert them to another king or prophet, nor can he conceal them.

Why Correlation Does Not Prove Prophethood
Critique of the first proof of prophethood using circular reasoning (dawran).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why correlation (dawran) is insufficient as proof for prophethood and how this objection undermines the first argument for prophecy.

Core Idea
Correlation alone does not prove causation; miracles coinciding with claims yield only weak conjecture.

This passage critiques the initial proof of prophethood that relies on temporal correlation. The author argues that simply observing a miracle at the time of a claim does not establish a necessary connection. Through an anecdote and logical reasoning, he demonstrates that correlation yields only weak conjecture, not certainty, and that analogies without a shared essential attribute are even weaker.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the original Arabic text and its English translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the structure: a summary statement, an illustrative story, then a logical extension.
The passage is dense; reading both versions will help.
2
Identify the core objection: criticism of dawran
The main claim is that the proof only shows a correlation (the miracle occurs when the claim is made, not before). Correlation alone does not establish causation or truthfulness.
The author uses the term 'al-dawran' to refer to this circular or correlational reasoning.
3
Understand the anecdote's role
The story of the muezzin and the man illustrates that two events may coincide without one causing the other. It serves as a counterexample to show that correlation is weak evidence.
This anecdote is a logical tool to demonstrate coincidental correlation.
4
Follow the extension to analogy without commonality
The author then argues that even if intellectual affirmation were granted, it cannot be assumed to hold for God. Analogy without a shared essential feature (jami') is even weaker than correlation.
This strengthens the critique by showing additional reasons the proof fails.
5
Grasp the overall conclusion
The passage concludes that the first proof of prophethood is invalid due to reliance on weak conjecture. Recognize how this fits into broader theological debates.
This conclusion is a necessary step in the author's larger argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فليس هنا إلا الدوران ، وهو أنه قام عند التماس المدعى ، وما قام قبل ذلك، والدوران لا يفيد إلا الظن الضعيف ، فإنه يحكى أن واحدا كان يجلس في مسجد فكلما دخل المؤذن قام ذلك الإنسان وخرج ، فقال له المؤذن مالى أراك كلما أذنت خرجت فقال لابل كلما هممت بالخروج أذنت، وهذا يدل على أن دلالة الدوران على العلمية ضعيف، ثم إن سلمنا دلالة العقل على التصديق فلم قلت إنه فى حق الله تعالى كذلك وستعرف أن القياس المؤيد بالجامع لا يفيد إلا الظن فكيف هذا القياس الخالى عن الجامع، فهذا هو الاعتراض على الدليل الأول على النبوة.
In summary, there is nothing here but correlation (al-dawran), and it is that it occurs at the time of the claimant's demand, and it did not occur before that. Correlation yields only weak conjecture (al-zann al-da'if). For it is narrated that a man was sitting in a mosque, and whenever the muezzin entered, that man stood up and left. The muezzin said to him: 'What is it that I see you leaving every time I call the adhan?' He replied: 'Rather, every time I intend to leave, you call the adhan.' This shows that the indication of correlation for certainty is weak. Then, if we grant the intellect's indication of affirmation, why did you say that it is likewise with respect to God? And you will know that analogy supported by a commonality (al-qiyas al-mu'ayyad bi al-jami') yields only conjecture, so how about this analogy devoid of commonality? This is the objection to the first proof of prophethood.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage critiques the first proof of prophethood by attacking its reliance on correlation (dawran). The author argues that the proof only establishes that the claimed miracle occurred at the time of the claimant's demand, not that it truly confirms prophethood. Correlation yields only weak conjecture, not certainty. The anecdote of the muezzin and the man illustrates that correlation can be coincidental. Even if the intellect's indication of truthfulness is accepted, the argument does not extend to God's case. Furthermore, analogies that lack a common feature are even weaker. Thus, the first proof is invalid.

Key Terms
الدوران (al-dawran)
الظن الضعيف (al-zann al-da'if)
القياس المؤيد بالجامع (al-qiyas al-mu'ayyad bi al-jami')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal correlation between a miracle and a prophetic claim is sufficient to prove the truthfulness of the claimant.
Correction: Correlation can be coincidental (as shown in the anecdote) and yields only weak conjecture, not certainty. The proof requires a necessary causal connection, which correlation does not provide.

Checkpoint
What does the anecdote of the muezzin and the man illustrate about correlation (dawran)?
A) Correlation proves causation.
B) Correlation can be coincidental and yields only weak conjecture.
C) Correlation is the strongest form of proof.
D) Correlation always leads to certain knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The first proof of prophethood fails because it relies on correlation (dawran), which yields only weak conjecture and does not establish necessary connection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a pattern of events in your life — a difficulty followed by a relief — do you automatically assume a causal link (e.g., 'I prayed and then it got better')? How might that assumption be a form of weak conjecture that prevents you from seeing Allah's wisdom more deeply? What would it take to examine your assumptions honestly?
?
The man in the anecdote reversed the causality to avoid blame. Can you think of a time when you reversed causality in your spiritual life — attributing a good outcome to your own actions while ignoring Allah's decree? How would you begin to correct that tendency today?
?
How often do you base a belief about Allah or His prophets on mere correlation rather than clear, certain proof? Are there any such beliefs you hold that you have not critically examined? Will you commit to testing them against the rigorous standards laid out in this lesson?

Critique of the Moral Excellences Proof
Critique of the second proof of prophecy based on the prophet's moral excellences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why moral excellences alone are insufficient as proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
Moral excellence proves virtue, not prophethood.

The second proof for prophethood rests on observing the prophet's noble character. But is that unique to prophets? This passage from Imam al-Razi challenges that assumption and shows why virtuous philosophers also displayed moral excellence and knowledge, yet were not prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the source text carefully, then its translation. Notice the concise style of critique.
The passage is a single sentence in Arabic.
2
Identify the second proof
Understand that the proof under critique is based on the prophet's moral excellences (mahasīn al-aḥwāl).
3
Analyze the weakness
The author argues that moral virtue only shows distinction, not prophethood. Ask yourself: what additional factor would be needed?
4
Consider the counterexample
Philosophers also had moral excellences and knowledge. Does that make them prophets? This shows the proof is insufficient.
5
Reflect on implications
Think about what unique signs are necessary to prove prophethood. This critique helps refine the criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ . الدليل الثانى وهو الاستدلال بمحاسن أحواله على نبوته فضعيف ، لأن غاية ما في الباب أنه يدل على كون ذلك الإنسان متميزا عن سائر الناس بمزيد الفضيلة ، ولكن من أين يدل على النبوة وكيف وقد حكى عن أفاضل الحكماء في الأخلاق أمور عجيبة جعلها الناس قدوة لأنفسهم في الدنيا والآخرة مع ما نقل عنهم من العلوم الدقيقة .
The second proof: relying on his noble qualities for his prophethood is weak, because the most it shows is that that person is distinguished from other people by extra virtue. But where does it indicate prophethood? And how, when wondrous things have been reported about the virtuous philosophers in ethics that people have made models for themselves in this world and the Hereafter, along with the precise sciences transmitted from them.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage critiques the second proof of prophethood, which argues from the prophet's moral excellences. The author considers this argument weak because it at most shows that the person is virtuous and distinguished, but many philosophers also exhibited admirable moral qualities and transmitted precise knowledge, yet they were not prophets. Thus, moral excellence alone does not prove prophethood.

Key Terms
محاسن الأحوال
الفضيلة
أفاضل الحكماء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that extraordinary moral character necessarily indicates prophethood.
Correction: Moral excellences are not exclusive to prophets; many philosophers also displayed them, so they do not prove prophethood.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the second proof (based on moral excellences) weak?
A) Because the prophet had no moral excellences.
B) Because many virtuous philosophers also had moral excellences and precise knowledge, yet were not prophets.
C) Because moral excellences are irrelevant to prophethood.
D) Because the proof relies on circular reasoning.

Key Takeaway
Moral virtues alone do not prove prophethood, as many philosophers also exhibited them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that someone's impressive character means they are divinely guided? How does this passage challenge that assumption? Will you now test such claims against the actual proofs of prophethood, or will you continue to rely on feelings?
?
Think of a person you admire for their ethics. Could their virtues be from philosophy or innate goodness without them being a prophet? How does that affect your trust in them and your criteria for recognizing truth?
?
This lesson warns against conflating virtue with prophethood. Where in your life have you confused goodness with truth? What concrete step will you take today to distinguish between the two?

The Objection to the Scriptural Proof
Objection to scriptural proof for prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-fold objection to using biblical predictions as proof for Muhammad's prophethood.

Core Idea
Scriptural proofs are challenged: either too vague or corrupted, but not both.

Imam Razi first presents the objection to the third proof of prophethood derived from the Torah and Gospel. The objection argues that either the predictions are detailed but absent from the scriptures, or they are general and not specific to Muhammad. This sets up the need for a robust defense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options
The objection presents two possibilities: either the predictions are detailed and specific, or they are general without specifics. Identify both.
2
Understand the first option's refutation
If detailed, the Torah and Gospel are said to be empty of such details. The objection dismisses the claim of corruption as implausible for widely known books.
Note the comparison to the Quran's preservation.
3
Understand the second option's refutation
If general, even if granted, it does not prove prophethood because it could apply to any virtuous person, not necessarily Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدليل الثالث وهو الاستدلال بما جاء فى التوراة والإنجيل على نبوة محمد الله فالاعتراض عليه أنكم إما أن تقولوا أنه جاء في هذه الكتب صفة محمد ا. فى هذه الكتب صفة محمد على الله على سبيل التفصيل بمعنى أنه تعالى بين أنه سيجيء في السنة القابلية في البلدة العلانية شخص وصفه كذا وكذا فاعلموا أنه رسولى ، وأما أن لا يقولوا كذلك بل يقولوا إنه تعالى بين ذلك بيانا مجملا من غير تعيين الزمان والمكان والوصف ، فإن ادعيتم لأول فهو باطل ، لانا نج. التوراة والإنجيل خالية عنه. لا يقال اليهود والنصارى حرفوا هذين الكتابين ، لانا نقول هذان كتابان مشهوران في المشارق والمغارب ومثل هذا مما لا يصح تطرق التحريف إليه كما في القرآن ، وإن ادعيتم الثاني فبتقدير المساعدة عليه لا يدل ذلك على النبوة ، بل ربما دل على ظهور إنسان فاضل شريف ، أو إن دل على إذ ربما كان المبشر به إنسانا آخر (1) النبوة لكن لا يدل على نبوة محمد ... (1) أقول : هذا الذي ذكره كله بمنزلة شبه السوفسطائية فإن التمين الحاصل للعقلاء إذا قام إنسان على طريق مرضية عند الخواص والعوام وادعى أنه مبعوث من عند الله ، والدليل على صدق قولى أن الله تعالى يظهر على يدى أمرا خارقا للعادة فظهر ، وقال من لم يصدقنى بمثل ما ظهر على يدى وتحدى من عداه لا يزول بأمثال هذه الاحتمالات وقد أشار المصنف أيضاً إلى هذا المعنى في الجواب على ما سيأتى . وأما المذكور فى التوراة والإنجيل الداله على نبوته الا الله وعلى آله وصحبه فكثيرة يذكرها المصنفون من الواقفين عليها منها ما ذكر فى التوراة بعبارة تفسيرها هكذا جاء الرب من طور سينا وظهر بساعير وعلا بفاران ، وفى التوراة أن إسماعيل كان فى سرية فاران يعنى بادية العرب وذكر الواقفون على جبالها أن فاران فى طريق مكة قبل الدون بميلين ونصف وهو كان المنزل المسافرين على يسار الطريق من العراق إلى مكة ، ومنه ما جاء في السفر الخامس أن الرب قال لموسى « إنى مقهم لهم نبيا مثلك من بنى أخوتهم وأيما رجل لم يسمع كلماتي التي يؤديها من ذلك الرجل باسمى أنا أنتقم منه ، ومنها في السفر الأول لهاجر أنها تلد ويكون من ولدها من يده فوق الجميع ويد الجميع مبسوطة إلى بالخشوع، وأيضاً جاء فى الإنجيل فى الفصل الرابع عشر في إنجيل يوحنا أن المسيح قال إنى أسأل أبى أن يعطيكم فارقليطا آخر يكون معكم إلى الأبد روح الحق ، والفارقليط معناه كاشف الخفيات، وأمثال هذا في هذين الكتابين وفى كتب سائر الأنبياء التى عندهم كثير يطول الكتاب بذكرها ولا يقدر المخالف على دفعها أو صرفها إلى ملك أو نبي آخر ولا على أن يكتمها .
As for the third proof: relying on what came in the Torah and Gospel for the prophethood of Muhammad, the objection against it is that either you say that in these books the description of Muhammad came in detail, meaning that God stated that in such-and-such a year, in such-and-such a town, a person of such description will come, so know that he is My messenger; or you do not say that, but rather say that God stated that in a general way without specifying time, place, and description. If you claim the first, it is false, for we find the Torah and Gospel empty of it. It cannot be said that the Jews and Christians corrupted these two books, for we say these two books are well-known in the east and west, and such a thing cannot be subject to corruption like the Quran. And if you claim the second, even if granted, that does not indicate prophethood; perhaps it indicates the appearance of a virtuous noble person, or if it indicates, perhaps the one given good news is another person... [1] I say: All that he has mentioned is like sophistical doubts. Indeed, the certainty attained by rational people when a person arises on a path approved by elite and common, claiming to be sent from God, with proof of his truthfulness being that God manifests through him something extraordinary, and it appears, and he challenges others to produce the like, cannot be removed by such probabilities. The author has also indicated this meaning in the answer to come. [Continues with scriptural evidence]... And examples of this in these two books and in the books of the other prophets are many, the book becomes long with their mention, and the opponent cannot refute them or divert them to another king or prophet, nor can he conceal them.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents the objection to the third proof of prophethood from scriptural predictions, then refutes it. The objection states that if the predictions are detailed, the scriptures lack them; if general, they do not necessarily indicate prophethood. The refutation first argues that the scriptures are not corrupted, then provides specific examples from Torah and Gospel that point to Muhammad. A marginal commentary defends the strength of such evidence against sophistical doubts. The author concludes that the evidence is abundant and irrefutable.

Key Terms
التوراة والإنجيل
التحريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection to the scriptural proof?
A) The Torah and Gospel were completely lost.
B) Either the predictions are detailed but absent, or general but not specific to Muhammad.
C) Muhammad never claimed to fulfill scripture.
D) The Torah and Gospel were revealed after Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
The objection to scriptural proof rests on a dilemma: detailed predictions are missing, general predictions are inconclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection dismisses the corruption argument by saying the scriptures are too widely known. Have you ever relied on a weak argument to support your faith? What does this teach you about intellectual honesty in your da'wa?
?
The dilemma forces a choice: either the prophecies are detailed but missing, or general but inconclusive. How do you respond when faced with a sharp either/or regarding your beliefs? Will you ignore it or grapple with it? Write down one step you will take to strengthen your understanding of the proofs of prophethood this week.

Scriptural Evidence for Muhammad's Prophethood
Defense of scriptural proof with specific biblical examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize specific biblical passages used by Muslim scholars to support the prophethood of Muhammad.

Core Idea
The Torah and Gospel contain clear predictions that only apply to Muhammad.

After presenting the objection, Imam Razi counters with concrete examples from the Torah and Gospel that point to Muhammad. These include descriptions of his coming from Paran, the promise of a prophet like Moses, and the Paraclete in John's Gospel. The author asserts that such evidence cannot be refuted or diverted.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first example from Torah
Read the passage about 'Jah came from Sinai, appeared from Seir, and shone from Paran.' Note the identification of Paran with Mecca.
This is interpreted as describing the revelations to Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad respectively.
2
Examine the second example: prophet like Moses
God promises to raise a prophet like Moses from among the brethren. Muslims see this as referring to Muhammad, as Ishmaelites are brethren of Israelites.
3
Examine the third example: Hagar's prophecy
Hagar is told her son (Ishmael) will have a hand over everyone and everyone's hand will be extended to him in submission.
This is seen as a prophecy about the Islamic community's leadership.
4
Examine the Gospel example: Paraclete
Jesus promises a Paraclete (the Spirit of Truth). The author states 'Paraclete' means 'revealer of hidden things', understood as Muhammad.
Muslims often equate the Paraclete with Muhammad.
5
Understand the author's conclusion
Razi concludes that there are many such prophecies, and opponents cannot refute them or apply them to anyone else.
This is a strong rebuttal to the objection.

First Two Objections to Prophethood: Materialists and Deniers of Obligation
Enumeration of the first two objections to prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two classical objections to prophethood: the materialist denial of a free-acting Creator, and the denial of divine obligation.

Core Idea
Prophethood is challenged by both those who reject a personal Creator and those who question God's right to command.

The author has just argued for the necessity of prophethood, but now acknowledges that there are objections. He lists the first two: the materialists who deny God's freedom, and the deniers of obligation who question the very concept of divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the author's enumeration of objections.
2
Understand the materialist doubt
The author says materialists attack the concept of a free agent who knows and wills. Consider why this would be an objection to prophethood.
3
Understand the denial of obligation
Deniers claim prophets bring obligations, but obligation itself is questionable. Think about why this would undermine prophethood.
4
Reflect on the pattern
Notice that both objections target the nature of God and His relationship to creation, setting up the need for refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن ما ذكرتم يدل على النبوة لكن هنا ما يدل على القدح فيها وهو من وجوه . الأول : شبهة الدهرية وهى بالقدح في الفاعل المختار وإنكار كون الصانع عالما قادرا بالحركات مريدا . وثانيها : شبهة منكرى التكليف فإنهم يقولون الأنبياء إنما جاءوا من عند الله تعالى بالتكاليف لكن القول بالتكليف مجال .
We grant that what you mentioned indicates prophethood, but there are indications that disparage it, and they are of several types. First: the doubt of the materialists (al-dahriyya), which disparages the freely acting agent (al-fā'il al-mukhtār) and denies that the Maker is knowing, powerful, and willing through movements. Second: the doubt of the deniers of obligation (munkirī al-taklīf), for they say that the prophets only came from God with obligations, but the affirmation of obligation is [problematic].
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage begins the enumeration of objections to prophethood. The author first lists the doubt of the materialists (al-dahriyya) who deny a personal, willing Creator. Then he mentions the doubt of those who deny divine obligation (al-taklīf), arguing that prophets bring obligations, but the concept of obligation itself is questionable. These are the first two of several objections that will be addressed.

Key Terms
شبهة الدهرية (shubhat al-dahriyya)
الفاعل المختار (al-fā'il al-mukhtār)
منكرى التكليف (munkirī al-taklīf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the doubt of the deniers of obligation (munkirī al-taklīf) claim?
A) That prophets are unnecessary because God directly guides everyone.
B) That prophets came with obligations, but the concept of obligation is problematic.
C) That prophethood is impossible.
D) That prophets were actually magicians.

Key Takeaway
The first two objections to prophethood are from materialists who deny a free Creator and from those who deny the validity of divine obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The materialists deny that God is a free agent with will and power. In what areas of your life do you act as if God is not in control or does not have the power to change your circumstances? How does the first objection expose that hidden assumption?
?
When you encounter objections to prophethood, do you tend to dismiss them quickly without understanding their precise arguments? How does this lesson challenge you to take objections seriously in order to refute them properly?

The Brahmin Objection to Prophethood
Two-aspect Brahmin objection against prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part Brahmin objection that challenges the necessity of prophetic revelation based on the sufficiency of reason and the invalidity of miracles.

Core Idea
Reason alone suffices; miracles are invalid — that is the Brahmin challenge.

The Brahmins (Barāhima) present a sophisticated epistemological argument against the necessity of prophethood. They claim that if reason can determine good and evil, prophets are unnecessary, and if reason cannot, utility governs acceptance. Additionally, they argue that miracles, the standard proof of prophecy, are not valid indicators. This lesson unpacks their two-pronged attack.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first aspect
Focus on how the Brahmins argue that if reason knows good and evil, revelation is superfluous; if not, utility determines acceptance, not revelation.
Notice the branching logic based on whether reason can or cannot judge.
2
Understand the second aspect
Examine the claim that miracles are the only proof of prophethood, but miracles are invalid, so no prophet can be confirmed.
This attacks the very foundation of how prophethood is verified.
3
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this objection is radical: it denies the need for revelation entirely, relying only on reason and utility.
This is a key challenge that theologians must answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها: شبهة البراهمة وهى من وجهين . الأول : أن ما جاء به الرسول إن علم حسنه بالعقل كان مقبولا سواء ورد به الرسول، أولم يرد ، وإن علم قبحه بالمقل كان مردودا سواء ورد به الرسول أو لم يرد ، وإن لم يعلم لاحسنه ولا قبحه ، فإن كان في محل الحاجة حسن الانتفاع به سواء ورد به الرسول أولم يرد ولما تقرر فى العقل إن كان ما ينتفع به الإنسان وكان خاليا عن أمارة الضرر كان الانتفاع به حسنا ، وإن لم يكن فى محل الحاجة قبح الانتفاع به سواء ورد الرسول أولم يرد ، لأنه إقدام على ما يحتمل الضرر من غير حاجة أصلا . الثاني : أن دلالة النبوة ليس إلا المعجزة بالاتفاق لكنا بينا أن المعجز لا يدل البتة فامتنع الجرم بالصدق .
And third: the doubt of the Brahmins (al-Barāhima), and it is of two aspects. First: that which the messenger brought, if its goodness is known by reason, it is acceptable whether the messenger brought it or not; and if its evil is known by reason, it is rejected whether the messenger brought it or not; and if neither its goodness nor evil is known, then if it is in a place of need, it is good to benefit from it whether the messenger brought it or not, since it is established in reason that if something is beneficial to man and free from sign of harm, benefiting from it is good; and if it is not in a place of need, benefiting from it is evil whether the messenger brought it or not, because it is an undertaking of something possibly harmful without any need. Second: that the indication of prophethood is only the miracle by agreement, but we have shown that the miracle does not indicate at all, so affirming truthfulness is impossible.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the two aspects of the Brahmin objection. First, they argue that if reason can determine good and evil, prophetic revelation is superfluous; if reason cannot, then need-based utility determines acceptance, not revelation. Second, they argue that only miracles indicate prophethood, but miracles are not valid proofs, thus no one can be confirmed as truthful. This is a radical epistemological challenge to the necessity of prophecy.

Key Terms
البراهمة (al-Barāhima)
حسن وقبح (ḥusn wa qubḥ)
المعجزة (al-mu'jiza)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Brahmins simply reject revelation without argument.
Correction: They actually present a reasoned objection based on the sufficiency of reason and the invalidity of miracles.

Checkpoint
What is the first aspect of the Brahmin objection regarding matters not known by reason?
A) They are always rejected.
B) If there is need, it is good to benefit from them regardless of revelation.
C) Only prophets can clarify them.
D) They are unknown and thus irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The Brahmin objection attempts to undermine the need for prophethood by asserting the sufficiency of reason and the failure of miraculous proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you made a decision relying solely on your own reason, ignoring what Allah has commanded? Did that decision lead to lasting peace or regret?
?
If the Brahmins are correct that miracles are not decisive proofs, what would be the foundation of your certainty that Muhammad (peace be upon him) is a true prophet? Do you have that certainty right now?
?
The Brahmins claim that if something is beneficial and harmless, we should accept it regardless of its source. Is there a truth you have rejected simply because it came from an unexpected person or tradition, without examining it? Are you willing to examine it now?

Prophethood

The Jewish Doubt: Objection Regarding Abrogation and Perpetuity
Fourth objection: Jews argue that Mosaic law is perpetual or temporally fixed, negating later prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jewish argument that the Mosaic law's stated duration (perpetual, temporally fixed, or unspecified) logically precludes the possibility of later prophethood.

Core Idea
The Jewish doubt challenges prophethood by analyzing the logical possibilities for Mosaic law's duration.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection from the Jewish tradition against the legitimacy of later prophets. It examines the implications of God's legislation for Moses: if the law was stated to be perpetual, abrogation would be impossible; if temporally fixed, the timing would be widely known; if unspecified, the law would not be binding continuously. Understanding this argument is key to engaging with interfaith theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense and uses conditional reasoning.
2
Identify the three possibilities for Mosaic law
The argument presents three options for the duration of Mosaic law: perpetual, temporally fixed, or unspecified. Find each in the text.
These correspond to 'ta'bīd', 'tawqīt', and lack of specification.
3
Understand the consequences of each possibility
For each option, the argument shows a contradiction or impossibility. Notice how the reasoning unfolds for the perpetual case (three subpoints), the fixed case (tawātur argument), and the unspecified case (reference to uṣūl al-fiqh).
The passage is cut off before completing the third case.
4
Grasp the overall aim of the objection
The Jewish objection aims to show that later prophets like Jesus and Muhammad cannot be legitimate because the Mosaic law's status precludes abrogation or change.
This is not just a claim but a structured logical argument.
5
Reflect on why this matters for prophethood
Consider how this argument challenges the Islamic belief in succession of prophets and why Islamic scholars would need to respond to it.
This prepares you for the counter-arguments that follow in the text.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : شبهة اليهود وهى من وجهين . الأول : الله تعالى لما شرع شريعة موسى عليه السلام فإما أن يكون قد بين فيها أن تكون باقية إلى يوم القيامة ، أو بين فيها أنها باقية إلى الوقت الفلاني فقط أو بين الشرع ولم يتعرض لبيان التأييد والأفيت . فإن قلت : إنه تعالى بين التأبيد لم يجز نسخه أما أولا فلانه أخبر أن هذا الشرع ثابت أبدا فلو لم يبق ثابتا أبدا كان كذبا وهو غير جائز على الله تعالى ، وأما ثانيا فلانه لو جاز أن ينص الله تعالى على أن شرع موسى عليه السلام ثابت أبدا ثم أنه لا يبقى ثابتا أبدا، فلم لا يجوز أن ينص الله على شرع محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه ثابت أبدا مع أنه لا يكون ثابتا أبدا فيلزمكم تجويز نسخ شرعكم . وأما ثالثا : فلانه لو جاز أن يخبر الله تعالى عن التأبيد مع أن التأبيد لا يحصل ارتفع الـ الأمان ووعده ووعيده وذلك باطل بالاتفاق . وأما إن قلنا إنه تعالى بين في شرع موسى عليه السلام أنه ثابت إلى الوقت الفلاني كان هذا من الأمور العظيمة التي تتوفر الدواعي على نقله فوجب أن ينقل ذلك التوقيت متواترا ، فالنقل المتواتر لا يجوز الإطباق على إخفائه فكان يلزم أن يكون العلم بانتهاء شرع موسى عند مبعث عيسى ، وانتهاء شرع عيسى عند مبحث محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام معلوما بالضرورة للخلق ، وأن يكون المنكر له منكراً للتواتر ، وأن يكون ذلك من أقوى الدلائل لعيسى ومحمد على دعواهما ، فلما لم يكن الأمر كذلك، علينا فساد هذا القسم ، ولأنه لو جاز أن لا ينقل هذا التوقيت نقلا متواترا الجاز أن يقال إن محمدا عليه الصلاة والسلام حول الصوم من رمضان إلى شوال ، والقبلة من الكعبة إلى غيرها ، ولأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال شرعى يبقى مؤ لداً إلى الوقت الفلاني مع أنه نه لم ينقل شيء من ذلك وتجويزه قدح فى شرع محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام ، وإن قلنا إنه تعالى تعالى بين في شرع أنه ثابت ولم يبين التأييد ولا التوقيت فهذا محال لما بينا، في كتاب أصول الفقه أن الأمر لا يفيد الوجوب إلا مرة واحدة، ومعلوم أن شرع موسى لم يكن كذلك فان التكاليف متوجهة بذلك الشرع على الخلق إلى [موسى]
And fourth: the doubt of the Jews, and it is of two aspects. First: When God legislated the law of Moses, either He stated in it that it would remain until the Day of Resurrection, or He stated that it would remain only until a certain time, or He legislated it without addressing perpetuity or termination. If you say that He stated perpetuity, then abrogation is not permissible. First, because He reported that this law is forever, so if it does not last forever, it would be a lie, which is not permissible for God. Second, because if it were permissible for God to state that the law of Moses is forever and then it does not last forever, then why is it not permissible for God to state that the law of Muhammad is forever while it does not last forever, thus you would have to allow abrogation of your own law. Third, because if it were permissible for God to report perpetuity while perpetuity does not occur, trust in His promise and threat would be undermined, which is unanimously false. And if we say that God stated in the law of Moses that it remains until a certain time, then this would be a great matter that would motivate widespread transmission, so that timing would have to be transmitted tawātur. Tawātur cannot be altogether concealed. Thus knowledge of the end of Mosaic law at Jesus' mission, and the end of Jesus' law at Muhammad's mission, would be necessarily known to all, and its denier would be a denier of tawātur, and it would be among the strongest proofs for Jesus and Muhammad. Since this is not the case, this division is invalid. Also, if it were permissible for this timing not to be transmitted tawātur, it would be permissible to say that Muhammad changed fasting from Ramadan to Shawwal, and the qibla from the Kaaba to elsewhere, and because the Prophet said 'My law remains forever until a certain time' while nothing of that has been transmitted, and allowing that impugns the law of Muhammad. And if we say that God stated in the law that it is established without specifying perpetuity or timing, this is impossible for what we have shown in the book of uṣūl al-fiqh that the command indicates obligation only once, and the law of Moses was not like that, for obligations continued by that law until...
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the Jewish objection in two aspects. The first aspect examines three possibilities for the Mosaic law's duration: perpetual, temporally fixed, or unspecified. If perpetual, abrogation is impossible on several grounds. If temporally fixed, the timing would be widely known, but it is not, so this fails. If unspecified, the law would not be binding continuously, contradicting its known application. This objection aims to show that later prophets (Jesus and Muhammad) cannot be legitimate because the Mosaic law's status precludes abrogation or change. The text cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms

التأبيد (al-ta'bīd)
التوقيت (al-tawqīt)
التواتر (al-tawātur)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jewish objection is simply a stubborn rejection of later prophets without reasoning.
Correction: The passage shows a detailed logical argument analyzing the implications of the Mosaic law's duration to conclude that later prophethood is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the Mosaic law was stated to be perpetual, what is one reason that would make abrogation impossible?
A) It would violate the principle of tawātur.
B) It would imply that God lied.
C) It would require a new prophet.
D) It would be hidden from the community.

Key Takeaway
The Jewish objection uses logical analysis of the Mosaic law's duration to argue that later prophethood is impossible, forcing Islamic scholars to respond with their own arguments about abrogation and divine wisdom.
