The Barahima's First Argument: Reason Suffices for Moral Knowledge
The Barahima's first shubha: reason suffices for moral discernment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Barahima's argument that if reason can determine moral value, then prophetic revelation is unnecessary.

Core Idea
If reason knows good and evil, prophets are redundant.

The Barahima, an ancient Indian school, famously denied the necessity of prophethood. Their first argument targets the very basis of moral knowledge. They claim that if reason can independently determine the goodness or evil of actions, then there is no need for a messenger to inform us. Imam Razi presents this objection here as a prelude to refuting it.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three cases
The passage presents three scenarios: known good, known evil, and unknown. Find each and notice how the Barahima conclude that revelation is redundant in all cases.
3
Ask: why does this matter?
Ask yourself why this argument is a serious challenge to prophethood. The commentary will help you see its implications.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها: شبهة البراهمة وهي من وجهين. الأول: أن ما جاء به الرسول إن علم حسنه بالعقل كان مقبولا سواء ورد به الرسول أو لم يرد، وإن علم قبحه بالعقل كان مردودا سواء ورد به الرسول أو لم يرد، وإن لم يعلم حسنه ولا قبحه، فإن كان في محل الحاجة حسن الانتفاع به سواء ورد به الرسول أو لم يرد ولما تقرر في العقل إن كان ما ينتفع به الإنسان وكان خاليا عن أمارة الضرر كان الانتفاع به حسنا، وإن لم يكن في محل الحاجة قبح الانتفاع به سواء ورد الرسول أو لم يرد، لأنه إقدام على ما يحتمل الضرر من غير حاجة أصلا. الثاني: أن دلالة النبوة ليس إلا المعجزة بالاتفاق لكنا بينا أن المعجز لا يدل البتة فامتنع الجزم بالصدق.
And the third: the shubha of the Barahima, and it is from two aspects. The first: that what the messenger brought, if its goodness is known by reason, it is accepted whether the messenger brought it or not; and if its evil is known by reason, it is rejected whether the messenger brought it or not; and if its goodness nor evil is known, then if it is in a place of need, benefiting from it is good whether the messenger brought it or not—since it is established in reason that if something is beneficial to man and is free from an indication of harm, benefiting from it is good—and if it is not in a place of need, benefiting from it is evil whether the messenger brought it or not, because it is an undertaking of something that may involve harm without any need. The second: that the indication of prophethood is nothing but the miracle by consensus, but we have shown that the miracle does not indicate at all, so certainty of truthfulness is impossible.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the shubha of the Barahima (a group of Indian origin often associated with rejection of prophethood) in two aspects. The first aspect argues that if moral values (ḥusn and qubh) are known by reason, then a prophet's message is redundant—either reason already accepts or rejects it. If reason is neutral, then the action's benefit or harm determines its morality independently of revelation. The second aspect attacks the probative value of miracles: although miracles are the agreed-upon proof of prophethood, the Barahima claim to have demonstrated that miracles provide no indication at all, so certainty in the prophet's truthfulness is impossible. Imam Razi presents this as an objection to be refuted later.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first aspect of the Barahima's shubha, what determines the moral status of an action when reason is neutral?
A) The prophet's report
B) The action's benefit and harm, and whether there is a need
C) The action's compatibility with scripture
D) The action's frequency

Key Takeaway
The Barahima's first argument claims that reason already provides a complete moral framework, making prophetic revelation superfluous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you made your last major moral decision, did you consult revelation or solely rely on your own reasoning? If the latter, what does that say about your actual need for prophethood in daily life? Will you now commit to checking divine guidance before acting?
?
The Barahima argue that reason alone can judge even neutral actions by weighing need and harm. Think of a 'neutral' area in your life—entertainment, spending, speech—where you didn't consider revelation. Where has your independent reasoning led you astray? Are you willing to submit those areas to prophetic guidance starting today?
?
This argument reduces prophethood to redundancy. Have you ever behaved as if you don't need prophetic guidance, trusting your own moral instincts? Pause and identify one area where your instincts contradict the Prophet's ﷺ teaching. Will you repent and align with his guidance, even if your reason disagrees?

The Barahima's Second Argument: Miracles Do Not Prove Prophethood
The Barahima's second shubha: miracles provide no indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Barahima's claim that miracles cannot serve as proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
Miracles are not valid indicators of truth.

The Barahima's second attack targets the universally accepted proof of prophethood: miracles. They assert that miracles have no probative value, thereby undermining the only agreed-upon evidence for a prophet's claim. Imam Razi presents this objection before refuting it.

Guided Path
1
Read the short claim
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the structure: consensus on miracle as proof, but they claim it has no indication.
2
Understand the implication
Think about why this argument is devastating if accepted. Without miracle evidence, prophethood cannot be established.
This connects to the learning objective.

Jewish Shubha on the Perpetuity of Moses' Law
Razi presents the Jewish argument against abrogation via three temporal possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jewish shubha (objection) regarding the perpetuity of Moses' law and the three possible specifications of its duration, and why the Jews argue that abrogation is impossible.

Core Idea
The Jewish argument claims that if God declared Moses' law eternal, then abrogation would be impossible without contradiction.

Imam Razi presents the Jewish shubha, which is a sophisticated argument against the abrogation of previous laws. The argument examines three logical possibilities regarding how God specified the duration of Moses' law. By analyzing each, the Jews conclude that only eternal perpetuity is consistent, making abrogation impossible. Understanding this shubha is key to appreciating the Islamic response in later lessons.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the three possibilities introduced at the start.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the logic.
2
Examine the first possibility: eternal perpetuity
The first possibility is that God specified Moses' law as eternal. The Jews then give three reasons why abrogation would be impossible: it would imply falsehood, it would allow similar for Muhammad's law, and it would undermine trust in divine promises.
Focus on how each reason builds on the concept of God's truthfulness.
3
Examine the second possibility: limited time
If God specified a limited time, that timing would be a crucial matter requiring mutawatir transmission. Since it was not transmitted, this possibility is invalid.
Note the reasoning that such a momentous announcement would have reached everyone.
4
Examine the third possibility: no specification
If God neither specified perpetuity nor a time limit, then the law would not impose continuous obligation. But Moses' law was continuously binding until Jesus, so this possibility also fails. Thus only the first remains.
This uses a principle from usul al-fiqh about commands implying only single obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها: شبهة اليهود وهي من وجهين. الأول: الله تعالى لما شرع شريعة موسى عليه السلام فإما أن يكون قد بين فيها أن تكون باقية إلى يوم القيامة، أو بين فيها أنها باقية إلى الوقت الفلاني فقط أو بين الشرع ولم يتعرض لبيان التأييد والتوقيت. فإن قلت: إنه تعالى بين التأبيد لم يجز نسخه أما أولا فلانه أخبر أن هذا الشرع ثابت أبدا فلو لم يبق ثابتا أبدا كان كذبا وهو غير جائز على الله تعالى، وأما ثانيا فلانه لو جاز أن ينص الله تعالى على أن شرع موسى عليه السلام ثابت أبدا ثم أنه لا يبقى ثابتا أبدا، فلم لا يجوز أن ينص الله على شرع محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام أنه ثابت أبدا مع أنه لا يكون ثابتا أبدا فيلزمكم تجويز نسخ شرعكم. وأما ثالثا: فلانه لو جاز أن يخبر الله تعالى عن التأبيد مع أن التأبيد لا يحصل ارتفع الأمان ووعده ووعيده وذلك باطل بالاتفاق. وأما إن قلنا إنه تعالى بين في شرع موسى عليه السلام أنه ثابت إلى الوقت الفلاني كان هذا من الأمور العظيمة التي تتوفر الدواعي على نقله فوجب أن ينقل ذلك التوقيت متواترا، فالنقل المتواتر لا يجوز الإطباق على إخفائه فكان يلزم أن يكون العلم بانتهاء شرع موسى عند مبعث عيسى، وانتهاء شرع عيسى عند مبعث محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام معلوما بالضرورة للخلق، وأن يكون المنكر له منكراً للتواتر، وأن يكون ذلك من أقوى الدلائل لعيسى ومحمد على دعواهما، فلما لم يكن الأمر كذلك، علمنا فساد هذا القسم، ولأنه لو جاز أن لا ينقل هذا التوقيت نقلا متواترا لجاز أن يقال إن محمدا عليه الصلاة والسلام حول الصوم من رمضان إلى شوال، والقبلة من الكعبة إلى غيرها، ولأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال شرعى يبقى مؤبداً إلى الوقت الفلاني مع أنه لم ينقل شيء من ذلك وتجويزه قدح فى شرع محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام، وإن قلنا إنه تعالى بين في شرع أنه ثابت ولم يبين التأييد ولا التوقيت فهذا محال لما بينا في كتاب أصول الفقه أن الأمر لا يفيد الوجوب إلا مرة واحدة، ومعلوم أن شرع موسى لم يكن كذلك فان التكاليف متوجهة بذلك الشرع على الخلق إلى زمان عيسى عليه السلام بالاتفاق، فلما ظهر فساد القسمين الأخيرين ثبت الأول، ويلزم من صحة الأول امتناع النسخ.
And the fourth: the shubha of the Jews, and it is from two aspects. The first: When God Almighty legislated the law of Moses peace be upon him, either He specified in it that it would remain until the Day of Resurrection, or He specified that it would remain only until a certain time, or He legislated the law without addressing a statement of perpetuity or timing. If you say: He specified perpetuity, then its abrogation is not permissible. Firstly, because He informed that this law is eternally established, so if it does not remain eternally established, it would be a lie, and that is not permissible for God. Secondly, because if it were permissible for God to stipulate that the law of Moses is eternally established and then it does not remain eternally, then why is it not permissible for God to stipulate that the law of Muhammad (peace be upon him) is eternally established while it is not eternally established? This would necessitate your allowing the abrogation of your own law. Thirdly, because if it were permissible for God to inform about perpetuity while perpetuity does not occur, trust in His promise and threat would be negated, and that is invalid by consensus. And if we say that He specified in the law of Moses that it remains until a certain time, this would be among the great matters for which motives would prompt transmission, so that timing would have to be transmitted mutawatir (massively). Mutawatir transmission cannot be collectively concealed. Then knowledge of the end of Moses' law at the time of Jesus' mission, and the end of Jesus' law at the time of Muhammad's mission, would be necessarily known to people, and the denier of it would be a denier of tawatur, and that would be among the strongest proofs for Jesus and Muhammad for their claims. Since the matter is not so, we know the invalidity of this division. Also, because if it were permissible that this timing not be transmitted mutawatir, it would be permissible to say that Muhammad (peace be upon him) changed the fast from Ramadan to Shawwal, and the qibla from the Ka'ba to something else, and that he said 'my law remains eternally until such a time' while nothing of that was transmitted, and its permissibility is an attack on the law of Muhammad. And if we say that He specified in the law that it is established but did not specify perpetuity nor timing, this is impossible because we have shown in the book of Usul al-Fiqh that a command does not indicate obligation except once. And it is known that Moses' law was not like that, for the obligations were continuing by that law upon people until the time of Jesus by consensus. When the invalidity of the last two divisions became clear, the first is established, and from the validity of the first follows the impossibility of abrogation.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the first aspect of the Jewish shubha, arguing for the perpetuity of Moses' law and against its abrogation. The argument proceeds by examining three possibilities regarding God's specification of the law's duration: (1) He specified eternal perpetuity, (2) He specified a limited time, or (3) He specified neither. If (1), abrogation is impossible because it would imply falsehood, undermine trust in divine promises, and lead to a problematic parallel with Muhammad's law. If (2), the timing should have been massively transmitted (mutawatir), yet it is not, so this possibility fails. If (3), the law would not imply continuous obligation, but Moses' law did impose obligation continuously until Jesus, so this too fails. Therefore, only (1) remains, implying that abrogation of Moses' law is impossible.

Key Terms
شبهة اليهود
تأبيد
توقيت
نسخ
متواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Jews are arguing for all scriptures being eternal, but the focus is specifically on Moses' law.
Correction: The shubha is specifically about the perpetuity of Moses' law, not a general claim about all divine laws.

Checkpoint
According to the Jewish shubha's first aspect, what is the consequence if God specified a limited time for Moses' law?
A) That timing would be transmitted mutawatir
B) The law would be abrogated by Jesus
C) The law would be eternal
D) The law would be unknown

Key Takeaway
The Jewish shubha argues that the only consistent possibility is that God declared Moses' law eternal, which would make abrogation impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Razi shows that if Moses' law had a time limit, it would have been transmitted widely. What crucial truths about your religion have you neglected to learn or transmit to others? Are you responsible for passing on knowledge you possess?

The Jewish Tawatur Argument and Its Refutation
Imam Razi presents the Jewish shubha from tawatur, its objection, refutation, and final answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi responds to the Jewish argument from tawatur that Moses and Jesus claimed their laws are eternal, and why the ultimate proof of Muhammad's prophethood is the Qur'an itself.

Core Idea
The tawatur argument is set aside; the Qur'an itself is the decisive proof.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a sophisticated theological argument from the Jewish and Christian traditions. They claim that their scriptures are eternally valid based on mass transmission (tawatur). Imam Razi not only refutes the objections to their tawatur but then makes a crucial move: he grounds the proof of Muhammad's prophethood in the Qur'an itself, reducing other arguments to mere supplements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: claim, objection, refutation, final answer.
2
Identify the tawatur claim
The Jews and Christians claim that Moses and Jesus each said their laws are eternal, and this is transmitted by tawatur. Understand why this would be problematic for Islam if accepted.
Tawatur is a key concept in Islamic epistemology for certain knowledge.
3
Examine the objection
The objector says the conditions of tawatur are not met because of historical events (Nebuchadnezzar's slaughter and early Christian scarcity). Note the specific argument.
This objection attempts to undermine the tawatur claim itself.
4
Study Razi's refutation
Imam Razi dismisses the objection as weak: the Jewish diaspora makes total annihilation impossible, and the Christian argument would attack Jesus' law before Muhammad.
5
Grasp the final answer
Despite seemingly defending the tawatur, Razi says the real proof of Muhammad's prophethood is the Qur'an. The other arguments are only for completion.
This is the decisive move – shifting from tawatur to the miracle of the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن اليهود والنصارى على كثرتهم وتفرقهم في المشارق والمغارب يخبرون عن موسى وعيسى عليهما الصلاة والسلام أن كل واحد منهما أخبر أن شريعته باقية إلى يوم القيامة وخبر التواتر يفيد العلم، وإلا لم يمكنكم إثبات وجود محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام فضلا عن نبوته، فإذا صح ذلك عنهما فلا شك أن قولهما حجة. لا يقال: شرط التواتر استواء الطرفين والواسطة وهذا مفقود في ذلك المعنى وهو النقل، أما اليهود فلأن بختنصر قتلهم حتى لم يبق منهم إلا عدد يسير دون عدد التواتر، وأما النصارى فلأنهم كانوا قليلين في ابتداء الأمر. لانا نقول أما قتل اليهود فضعيف لأن اليهود كانوا أمة عظيمة وكانوا متفرقين في شرق الأرض وغربها وفي البلاد المتباعدة جدا فمن المستحيل قتل هذه الأمة العظيمة بحيث لا يبقى على وجه الأرض منهم عدد التواتر، وأما حديث النصارى فضعيف أيضا لأن ذلك يوجب القدح في شرع عيسى عليه السلام قبل مبعث محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام وذلك مما لم يقل به أحد. والجواب: أن المعتمد في رسالة محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام ظهور القرآن عليه، وسائر الوجوه إنما يذكر للتتمة والتكميل.
The second: that the Jews and Christians, despite their number and dispersion in the East and West, report from Moses and Jesus (peace be upon them) that each of them informed that his law remains until the Day of Resurrection. The report of tawatur produces certain knowledge, otherwise you could not prove the existence of Muhammad (peace be upon him), let alone his prophethood. So if that is established from them, there is no doubt that their statement is authoritative. It cannot be said: the condition of tawatur is equality of the first, middle, and last generations, and this is lacking in that meaning, i.e., the transmission. As for the Jews, because Nebuchadnezzar killed them until only a small number remained below the number of tawatur. As for the Christians, because they were few at the beginning. We say: as for the killing of the Jews, it is weak because the Jews were a great nation and they were scattered in the East and West and in very distant lands, so it is impossible to kill this great nation such that not even the number of tawatur remains on the face of the earth. As for the account of the Christians, it is also weak because that would entail attacking the law of Jesus before the mission of Muhammad, and that is something no one has said. And the answer: that the main reliance in the mission of Muhammad (peace be upon him) is the appearance of the Qur'an upon him, and other aspects are only mentioned for completion and perfection.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the second aspect of the Jewish shubha, which argues that Jews and Christians across the world massively transmit (tawatur) that Moses and Jesus each declared their laws eternal. This tawatur produces certain knowledge, and if it is denied, then the existence of Muhammad himself cannot be proven through tawatur. An objection is raised that the condition of tawatur (equality of all links in the chain) is not met because the Jews were decimated by Nebuchadnezzar and the early Christians were few. Imam Razi responds by dismissing these objections: the Jewish scattering makes total annihilation impossible, and the Christian argument would undermine Jesus' law before Muhammad. Finally, he gives the overall answer: the real proof of Muhammad's prophethood is the Qur'an itself, and other arguments are supplementary.

النصارى
بختنصر
القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think Imam Razi accepts the Jewish tawatur claim, but he ultimately sets it aside in favor of the Qur'an.
Correction: Imam Razi does not concede the argument; he shifts the basis of proof entirely to the Qur'an.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Tawatur Argument for Eternal Laws and Its Refutation
Refutes the claim that tawatur establishes the permanence of Moses' and Jesus' laws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tawatur argument for the permanence of previous laws and the Islamic response that relies on the Qur'an as the primary proof.

Core Idea
The ultimate proof of Muhammad's prophethood is the Qur'an itself, not tawatur reports.

This passage presents an objection from Jews and Christians claiming that their laws are eternal based on mass transmission (tawatur), and then offers a refutation. It demonstrates how theological arguments must be critically examined and that the real evidence for Islam is the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage to understand the claim that tawatur establishes the permanence of Moses' and Jesus' laws.
Notice the assumption that tawatur provides certain knowledge.
2
Examine the counter-arguments
Identify the historical objections: Nebuchadnezzar's killing of Jews and the early scarcity of Christians.
These are meant to break the tawatur chain.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
See how the author dismisses those objections as weak, then provides the final answer: the real proof is the Qur'an.
Observe the shift from secondary arguments to the primary evidence.
4

زمان عيسى عليه السلام بالاتفاق ، فلما ظهر فساد القسمين الأخيرين ثبت الأول ، ويلزم من صحة امتناع النسخ. الثاني : أن اليهود والنصارى على كثرتهم وتفرقهم فى المشارق والمغارب يخبرون عن موسى وعيسى عليهما الصلاة والسلام أن كل واحد منهما أخبر أن شريعته باقية إلى يوم القيامة وخبر التواتر يفيد العلم ، وإلا لم يمكنكم إثبات وجود محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام فضلا عن نبوته ، فإذا صح ذلك عنهما فلاشك أن قولهما حجة لا يقال : شرط التواتر استواء الطرفين والواسطة وهذا مفقود فى ذلك المعنى وهو النقل، أما اليهود فلان بختنصر قتلهم حتى لم يدق منهم إلا عدد يسير دون عدد التواتر ، وأما النصارى فلانهم كانوا قليلين في ابتداء الأمر. لانا نقول أما قتل اليهود فضعيف لأن اليهود كانوا أمة عظيمة وكانوا متفرقين فى شرق الأرض وغربها وفى البلاد المتباعدة جدا فمن المستحيل قتل هذه الأمة العظيمة بحيث لا يبقى على وجه الأرض منهم عدد التواتر ، وأما حديث النصارى فضعيف أيضا لأن ذلك يوجب القدح في شرع عيسى عليه السلام قبل مبعث محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام وذلك مما لم يقل به أحد والجواب : أن المعتمد في رسالة محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام ظهور القرآن عليه ، وسائر الوجوه إنما يذكر للتتمة والتكميل .
The time of Jesus (peace be upon him) by agreement. When the corruption of the last two divisions became apparent, the first was established, and the validity of the impossibility of abrogation follows. Second: The Jews and Christians, despite their multitude and dispersion in the East and West, report about Moses and Jesus (peace be upon them) that each one of them informed that his law remains until the Day of Resurrection, and the report of tawatur (متواتر) yields knowledge. Otherwise, you could not prove the existence of Muhammad (peace be upon him) let alone his prophethood. So if that is established from them, there is no doubt that their statement is a proof. It cannot be said: the condition of tawatur is that the two ends and the middle should be equal, and this is missing in that meaning, i.e., the transmission. As for the Jews, Nebuchadnezzar killed them until only a small number remained, less than the number of tawatur. As for the Christians, they were few at the beginning. We say: As for the killing of the Jews, it is weak because the Jews were a great nation, dispersed in the East and West of the earth and in far apart lands, so it is impossible to kill this great nation leaving no one on the face of the earth equal to the number of tawatur. As for the matter of the Christians, it is also weak because that would entail disparagement of the law of Jesus before the advent of Muhammad (peace be upon him), and that is something no one has said. The answer: what is relied upon for the message of Muhammad (peace be upon him) is the appearance of the Qur'an as a miracle, and other aspects are only mentioned for completion and perfection.

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that the laws of Moses and Jesus are eternal based on tawatur reports that they said so. The responder points out that the tawatur is broken by historical events, but the answer refutes that by showing the weakness of those objections, then concludes that the real proof of Muhammad's prophethood is the Qur'an itself.

Three Rational Excuses Prophets Remove
Enumerates three rational excuses that prophets remove.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three excuses that prophets address according to rational theology.

Core Idea
Prophets cut off every excuse reason might offer.

Imam Razi begins by dividing matters into those reason can perceive and those it cannot. For the former, prophets reinforce reason and remove excuses. Scholars identified three specific excuses that prophets eliminate, based on human need for guidance, weakness against desires, and uncertainty about eternal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division of matters
Read the opening lines where the author states that matters are of two types: those reason can perceive and those it cannot. Notice that the first type includes knowledge of the world's need for a Creator.
This sets up the context for the three excuses.
2
Identify the three excuses
The passage lists three excuses that people might offer if prophets were not sent. Find each one and note how it addresses a specific human limitation.
First: need for detailed worship. Second: forgetfulness and desires. Third: unknown reward and punishment.
3
See how prophets cut off each excuse
For each excuse, the author explains how the arrival of prophets removes that excuse. Reflect on the Quranic verse cited: 'So that mankind should have no argument against God after the messengers.'
Prophets provide detailed laws, reminders, and knowledge of the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع وأما شبهة البراهمة فهي مبنية على الحسن و القبح وقد تقدم القول فيه . ولنذكر فوائد البعثة على التفصيل فنقول : قد عرفت أن الأمور قسمان منها ما يستقل العقل بإدراكه ومنها ما لا يستقل والأول كلمنا بافتقار العالم إلى الصانع الحكيم وفائدة بعثة الرسل في هذا تأكيد العقل بدليل النقل وقطع عذر المكلام من كل الوجوه على ما قال تعالى : لئلا يكون للناس على الله حجة بعد الرسل ( وقال ولو أنا أهلكناهم بعذاب من قبله لقالوا ربنا لولا أرسلت إلينا رسولا فتتبع آياتك من قبل أن نذل وتخزى فبين تعالى أن بعثة الرسل القطع الحجة والعلماء ذكروا وجرها ثلاثة . الأول : أن قالوا إن الله تعالى إن كان خلقنا لنعبده فقد كان يجب أن يبين لنا العبادة التي يريدها منا أنها ما هى وكم هى وكيف هى ، فإن وجبت أصل الطاعة فى العقل لكن كيفيتها غير معلومة لنا فبعث الله تعالى الرسل لقطع هذا العذر فإنهم إذا بينوا الشرائع المفصلة زالت أعذارهم . وثانيها : أن يقولوا إنك ركبتنا تركيب سهو وغفلة وسلطت علينا الهوى والشهوات فهلا أمددتنا يا إلهنا من إذا سهونا نبهنا وإذا مال بنا الهوى منعنا ولكنك ما تركتنا مع نفوسنا وأهوائنا كان ذلك إغراء لنا على تلك القبائح . وثالثها : أن يقولوا : هب أنا بعقولنا علمنا حسن الإيمان وقبح الكفران ، ولكن لا نعلم بعقولنا أن من فعل القبح عذب خالدا مخلدا في النار ، لا سيما وأنا تعلم أن لنا في القبح لذة وليس لك فيه مضرة، ولم نعلم أن من آمن وعمل صالحا استحق النواب الخالد لا سيما وكنا قد علمنا أنه لا منفعة لك في شيء فلا حرم لم يكن مجرد العلم بالحسن و القبح داعيا ولا وازها . أما بعد البعثة اندفعت هذه الأعذار فكانت البعثة قطعا لعذر المعذرين من هذه الوجوه ، وأما فائدة بعثهم فيما لا يستقل العقل بدركه فقد ذكروا أمورا . أحدها : أن العقل لا يدل إلا على الصفات التي يحتاج إليها ، أما السمع والبصر والكلام وسائر الصفات الجزئية فلا طريق إليها إلا السمع .
Section: As for the doubt of the Brahmans, it is based on good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ) and has been discussed before. Let us mention the benefits of sending prophets (baʿtha) in detail. We say: You have known that matters are of two types: that which the intellect (al-ʿaql) can perceive independently, and that which it cannot. The first includes our knowledge of the world's need for a Wise Maker. The benefit of sending prophets in this is to reinforce the intellect with transmitted proof (naql) and to cut off the excuse of the responsible person (mukallaf) in every way, as God said: 'So that mankind should have no argument against God after the messengers' (Quran 4:165) and He said: 'And if We had destroyed them with a punishment before him, they would have said: Our Lord, why did You not send us a messenger so we could follow Your verses before we are humiliated and disgraced?' (Quran 20:134). So God made it clear that sending prophets cuts off the argument. The scholars mentioned three aspects. First: They said: If God created us to worship Him, He must show us the worship He wants from us: what it is, how much, and how. For the obligation of obedience in principle is known by reason, but its modality is unknown to us. So God sent prophets to cut off this excuse, for when they explain the detailed laws, the excuses are removed. Second: They say: You composed us with forgetfulness and inattention, and You gave power to whims and desires. So why did You not supply us, O our God, with someone who would remind us when we forget and prevent us when our whims incline, but You left us to ourselves and our desires? That would be an incitement to those evil deeds. Third: They say: Suppose we know by our intellects the goodness of faith and the evil of disbelief, but we do not know by our intellects that one who does evil will be punished eternally in the Fire, especially since we know that we have pleasure in evil and You have no harm in it, and we do not know that one who believes and does good deeds deserves eternal reward, especially since we knew that there is no benefit to You in anything, so neither mere knowledge of good and evil would be a motive nor a deterrent. After the sending of prophets, these excuses were removed, so the sending cut off the excuses of the excuse-makers in these aspects. As for the benefit of their sending in matters that the intellect does not perceive independently, they mentioned things. One of them: The intellect only indicates the attributes that are necessary, but hearing, sight, speech, and other particular attributes (ṣifāt juzʾiyya) have no path to them except revelation (samʿ).
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a classification and enumeration of the benefits of sending prophets. The author divides matters into those known by reason and those not. For the former, prophets reinforce reason and cut off excuses. Three excuses are listed: (1) need for detailed worship instructions, (2) need for external aid against forgetfulness and desires, (3) need to know reward and punishment beyond reason. For the latter (matters beyond reason), one example is that reason only knows necessary attributes but not detailed attributes like hearing, sight, etc., which require revelation.

Key Terms

عقل
حجة
حسن وقبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think reason alone can provide all religious guidance.
Correction: The passage shows reason is insufficient for details and eternal consequences; prophets are needed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first excuse that prophets remove?
A) Humans need to know the detailed forms of worship
B) Humans are forgetful and need reminders
C) Humans cannot know reward and punishment
D) Humans need to know God's essence

Key Takeaway
Prophets cut off three excuses: ignorance of worship details, weakness against desires, and uncertainty about the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How does prophecy confirm this truth and remove that excuse? Will you now live with the certainty that your actions have eternal weight?

Revelation Needed for Divine Attributes
States that reason cannot know particular attributes without revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why revelation is needed for knowing certain attributes of God.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot know all divine attributes.

After discussing matters reason can know, Imam Razi turns to matters beyond reason's independent grasp. The intellect only indicates necessary attributes, but particular attributes like hearing, sight, and speech require revelation. This establishes a key limitation of reason and the necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on matters beyond reason
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the author's statement that the intellect only indicates necessary attributes.
The term 'samʿ' (revelation) is used as the source for particular attributes.
2
Identify the limitation of reason
The passage states that reason cannot perceive certain attributes independently. Which attributes are mentioned? Hearing, sight, speech, and other particular attributes.
These are called 'ṣifāt juzʾiyya' (particular attributes).
3
Understand the role of revelation
Revelation (samʿ) provides knowledge of these attributes. Reflect on why this shows the need for prophets.
Prophets bring revelation that informs us of God's attributes beyond what reason can deduce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع وأما شبهة البراهمة فهي مبنية على الحسن و القبح وقد تقدم القول فيه . ولنذكر فوائد البعثة على التفصيل فنقول : قد عرفت أن الأمور قسمان منها ما يستقل العقل بإدراكه ومنها ما لا يستقل والأول كلمنا بافتقار العالم إلى الصانع الحكيم وفائدة بعثة الرسل في هذا تأكيد العقل بدليل النقل وقطع عذر المكلام من كل الوجوه على ما قال تعالى : لئلا يكون للناس على الله حجة بعد الرسل ( وقال ولو أنا أهلكناهم بعذاب من قبله لقالوا ربنا لولا أرسلت إلينا رسولا فتتبع آياتك من قبل أن نذل وتخزى فبين تعالى أن بعثة الرسل القطع الحجة والعلماء ذكروا وجرها ثلاثة . الأول : أن قالوا إن الله تعالى إن كان خلقنا لنعبده فقد كان يجب أن يبين لنا العبادة التي يريدها منا أنها ما هى وكم هى وكيف هى ، فإن وجبت أصل الطاعة فى العقل لكن كيفيتها غير معلومة لنا فبعث الله تعالى الرسل لقطع هذا العذر فإنهم إذا بينوا الشرائع المفصلة زالت أعذارهم . وثانيها : أن يقولوا إنك ركبتنا تركيب سهو وغفلة وسلطت علينا الهوى والشهوات فهلا أمددتنا يا إلهنا من إذا سهونا نبهنا وإذا مال بنا الهوى منعنا ولكنك ما تركتنا مع نفوسنا وأهوائنا كان ذلك إغراء لنا على تلك القبائح . وثالثها : أن يقولوا : هب أنا بعقولنا علمنا حسن الإيمان وقبح الكفران ، ولكن لا نعلم بعقولنا أن من فعل القبح عذب خالدا مخلدا في النار ، لا سيما وأنا تعلم أن لنا في القبح لذة وليس لك فيه مضرة، ولم نعلم أن من آمن وعمل صالحا استحق النواب الخالد لا سيما وكنا قد علمنا أنه لا منفعة لك في شيء فلا حرم لم يكن مجرد العلم بالحسن و القبح داعيا ولا وازها . أما بعد البعثة اندفعت هذه الأعذار فكانت البعثة قطعا لعذر المعذرين من هذه الوجوه ، وأما فائدة بعثهم فيما لا يستقل العقل بدركه فقد ذكروا أمورا . أحدها : أن العقل لا يدل إلا على الصفات التي يحتاج إليها ، أما السمع والبصر والكلام وسائر الصفات الجزئية فلا طريق إليها إلا السمع .
Section: As for the doubt of the Brahmans, it is based on good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ) and has been discussed before. Let us mention the benefits of sending prophets (baʿtha) in detail. We say: You have known that matters are of two types: that which the intellect (al-ʿaql) can perceive independently, and that which it cannot. The first includes our knowledge of the world's need for a Wise Maker. The benefit of sending prophets in this is to reinforce the intellect with transmitted proof (naql) and to cut off the excuse of the responsible person (mukallaf) in every way, as God said: 'So that mankind should have no argument against God after the messengers' (Quran 4:165) and He said: 'And if We had destroyed them with a punishment before him, they would have said: Our Lord, why did You not send us a messenger so we could follow Your verses before we are humiliated and disgraced?' (Quran 20:134). So God made it clear that sending prophets cuts off the argument. The scholars mentioned three aspects. First: They said: If God created us to worship Him, He must show us the worship He wants from us: what it is, how much, and how. For the obligation of obedience in principle is known by reason, but its modality is unknown to us. So God sent prophets to cut off this excuse, for when they explain the detailed laws, the excuses are removed. Second: They say: You composed us with forgetfulness and inattention, and You gave power to whims and desires. So why did You not supply us, O our God, with someone who would remind us when we forget and prevent us when our whims incline, but You left us to ourselves and our desires? That would be an incitement to those evil deeds. Third: They say: Suppose we know by our intellects the goodness of faith and the evil of disbelief, but we do not know by our intellects that one who does evil will be punished eternally in the Fire, especially since we know that we have pleasure in evil and You have no harm in it, and we do not know that one who believes and does good deeds deserves eternal reward, especially since we knew that there is no benefit to You in anything, so neither mere knowledge of good and evil would be a motive nor a deterrent. After the sending of prophets, these excuses were removed, so the sending cut off the excuses of the excuse-makers in these aspects. As for the benefit of their sending in matters that the intellect does not perceive independently, they mentioned things. One of them: The intellect only indicates the attributes that are necessary, but hearing, sight, speech, and other particular attributes (ṣifāt juzʾiyya) have no path to them except revelation (samʿ).
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a classification and enumeration of the benefits of sending prophets. The author divides matters into those known by reason and those not. For the former, prophets reinforce reason and cut off excuses. Three excuses are listed: (1) need for detailed worship instructions, (2) need for external aid against forgetfulness and desires, (3) need to know reward and punishment beyond reason. For the latter (matters beyond reason), one example is that reason only knows necessary attributes but not detailed attributes like hearing, sight, etc., which require revelation.

Key Terms
عقل
سمع
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think reason can deduce all of God's attributes, including hearing, sight, and speech.
Correction: The passage clarifies that reason can only know necessary attributes; particular attributes require revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how can we know God's attribute of speech?
A) Through rational deduction
B) Through revelation
C) Through analogy with human speech
D) Through mystical experience

Key Takeaway
Revelation is necessary to know God's particular attributes like hearing, sight, and speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to understand God's nature using only your own reasoning? In what ways does this lesson expose the limits of that approach, and how can you intentionally prioritize revelation in your understanding of who God is?
?
When you read descriptions of God hearing your prayers or seeing your actions, do you truly accept them as real attributes, or do you subconsciously reduce them to metaphors because your reason struggles to conceive of them? How can you train your heart to submit to revelation over rational discomfort?
?
If you find yourself questioning a divine command because it seems 'unreasonable' according to your intellect, what would it look like to instead trust in the revelation that God has hearing, sight, and speech—attributes that surpass your full comprehension? Will you commit today to seeking those answers from scripture rather than your own logic?

Prophethood
beginner
4 min
The Second Argument: Fear of the Mukallaf
Second argument: the dilemma of the mukallaf removed by prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the necessity of prophethood, which is based on the psychological state of the morally responsible person (mukallaf) who remains in fear due to the absence of divine guidance.

Core Idea
Prophethood removes the dilemma of fear that reason alone cannot resolve.

The second argument for prophethood focuses on the inner turmoil of the morally responsible person. Without prophetic guidance, a person is caught in a dilemma: whether to act in worship without explicit permission or to refrain and risk punishment. This passage presents that argument concisely and shows how prophethood resolves the fear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the dilemma presented. Notice the two scenarios: acting in worship without permission and refraining with risk of punishment.
2
Identify the two options
The text outlines two possibilities: either the mukallaf engages in acts of obedience (طاعات) and fears acting without God's permission, or he does not engage and fears punishment for neglecting duty. Both lead to fear.
3
Reflect on the resolution
The argument concludes that prophethood (بعثة) removes this fear by providing clear commands, so the mukallaf knows what is required and can act with confidence.
This shows that prophethood is necessary for psychological and moral stability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : أن المكلف يبقى خائفا فيقول لو اشتغلت بالطاعات لكنت متصرف في ملك الله تعالى بغير إذنه ، ولو لم يشتغل بها فربما عذب على ترك الطاعة فيبقى فى الخريف على التقديرين ، وعند البعثة يزول هذا الخوف . وثالثها : أنه ليس كل ما كان قبيحا عندنا كان قبيحا في نفسه ؛ فإن النظر إلى وجه الحرة العجوز الشوهاء قبيح وإلى وجه الأمة الحسناء حسن
And the second [argument]: that the responsible person (mukallaf) remains fearful, saying: 'If I occupy myself with acts of obedience, I am acting in the dominion of God without His permission, and if I do not occupy myself with them, perhaps He will punish me for abandoning obedience,' so he remains in fear in both cases, and with the sending (of prophets) this fear disappears. And the third [argument]: that not everything that is ugly (qabih) in our view is ugly in itself; for looking at the face of a free, old, ugly woman is ugly, and looking at the face of a beautiful slave girl is beautiful.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents the second and third arguments for the necessity of prophethood. The second argument addresses the psychological state of the morally responsible person (mukallaf) before revelation. Without prophetic guidance, a person is caught in a dilemma: if they engage in acts of worship, they may be overstepping their bounds in God's dominion without explicit permission; if they refrain, they face possible punishment for neglecting duty. Thus they remain in constant fear. Prophethood removes this fear by providing clear commands. The third argument concerns the relativity of moral judgments. What we consider ugly (qabih) may not be intrinsically ugly. The example given is that looking at the face of a free, old, ugly woman is considered ugly, but looking at a beautiful slave girl is considered good. This shows that moral values are not inherent but depend on context and divine command, so revelation is needed to establish true moral standards.

Key Terms
مكلف
بعثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that fear alone justifies prophethood, but the argument is about an irresolvable dilemma that only divine guidance can solve.
Correction: The argument is not merely about fear, but about the logical impasse that reason cannot resolve without revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what is the state of the mukallaf without prophethood?
A) He is at peace because he follows reason.
B) He remains fearful in both action and inaction.
C) He only fears punishment if he disobeys.
D) He is certain of salvation through good deeds.

Key Takeaway
Without prophethood, the morally responsible person remains in an impossible dilemma of fear, which is only resolved by divine guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever faced a situation where you felt uncertain about whether an action was permissible or not, and you experienced the kind of fear described in this lesson? How did you resolve it, and what role did divine guidance play?
?
If you were left only with your reason and no revelation, which path would you choose—obedience with fear of overstepping, or abandonment with fear of punishment? Be honest: does this dilemma actually exist in your daily life?
?
This lesson says that prophethood removes fear. Is there an area of your life where you still carry this fear because you haven't fully embraced the clarity that revelation provides? What would it take for you to let that fear go and trust in the guidance you have been given?

The Third Argument: Relativity of Good and Evil
Third argument: moral values are relative and require revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the necessity of prophethood, which is that moral judgments (good and evil) are not absolute but depend on context and divine command.

Core Idea
What we consider good or evil depends on context, so we need prophets to know true moral values.

The third argument tackles the nature of moral values. It argues that what we deem ugly (qabih) or beautiful (hasan) is not intrinsic but relative to circumstances. This point underscores why we need prophetic revelation to establish objective moral standards, as reason alone cannot determine absolute good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the claim that not everything considered ugly in our view is intrinsically ugly, and the example provided.
2
Analyze the example
Contrast looking at the face of a free, old, ugly woman (considered ugly) versus looking at a beautiful slave girl (considered good). The moral judgment changes based on social and contextual factors, not inherent qualities.
This shows that our moral intuitions are relative.
3
Connect to prophethood
Understand that without divine revelation, we cannot know which actions are truly good or evil. Prophets bring clear commands that establish objective moral standards.
This argument supports the necessity of prophethood for moral knowledge.

Source Text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that good and evil are purely rational and universal, but this argument shows they are relative to custom or divine permission.
Correction: The argument demonstrates that moral judgments can vary based on context, so revelation is needed to know what is truly good or evil.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument use as an example to show that moral judgments are relative?
A) Lying and truthfulness
B) Looking at a free old woman vs. a beautiful slave girl
C) Stealing and charity
D) Prayer and fasting

Key Takeaway
Moral judgments are relative and context-dependent, so prophetic revelation is necessary to establish objective standards of good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you were absolutely certain an action was wrong, but later realized your judgment was shaped by your culture or upbringing. How does this argument challenge your confidence in your own moral intuition? What will you do to ensure your moral standards align with revelation rather than personal bias?
?
The example contrasts looking at an old free woman and a beautiful slave girl. Are there people in your life you treat differently based on their appearance, status, or age? How does this argument call you to examine your heart and correct any unfair judgments?
?
If moral standards are not inherent, then without prophetic guidance, how would you know right from wrong? Do you truly submit to revelation in your moral decisions, or do you still rely on your own reasoning? What is one area of your life where you need to align your judgments more closely with what Allah has revealed through His prophets?
